EMAIL 
was 
in 
my freshman year of college in 
1988. I attended Dartmouth, and 
we had a system named BlitzMail. 
Every student was granted an email 
address and access to the system. 
Like most students, I didn‚Äôt use it at 
all. It‚Äôs hard for even me to believe, 
but back then I was in the habit of 
writing letters and postcards.
I vividly recall the tipping point, 
though. In 1re years and my first job 
in PC Week‚Äôs lab in 1992 to actually 
start using it. Like a lot of corporate 
workplaces, email was commonly 
used for interoffice communications. 
But the volume wa989 in the middle 
of my sophomore year, I was eating 
lunch with a few freshmen who were 
talking about how great email was 
for chatting up women. I remember 
being shocked at the notion that 
these guys didn‚Äôt have the courage to 
pick up the phone. Little did I know. 
I‚Äôd be lying if I said that I took to 
email the next day. I didn‚Äôt. It took 
three more years and my first job 
in PC Week‚Äôs lab in 1992 to actually 
start using it. Like a lot of corporate 
workplaces, email was commonly 
used for interoffice communications. 
But the volume was manageable; I 
remember going two or three hours 
without new email. That sounds 
absolutely crazy now, but this was 
the era before SPAM even existed. 
As more friends started working 
and came online, the postcard- and 
letter-writing days ended forever. 
(That lab job was also awesome 
because after four years of using 
a Mac in college, I was able to get 
back to PC gaming‚Äîafter hours, of 
course.)
I‚Äôll be honest; I still love checking 
my inbox and discovering email‚Äî
from 
friends, 
family, 
writers, 
readers, PR people, whomever‚Äîeven 
if it has become a bit overwhelming. 
Between re years and my first job 
in PC Week‚Äôs lab in 1992 to actually 
start usingaccounts, I‚Äôve never had 
trouble keeping up. Until now. This is 
partially what inspired this month‚Äôs 
cover story. Over the last 18 months, 
I‚Äôve begun to notice that my ability to 
quickly parse, read, and respond to 
email just isn‚Äôt fast enough anymore. 
I need to develop new systems and 
shortcuts. I need to change my ways. 
I‚Äôm 
curious‚Äîare 
you 
finding 
yourself in the same boat as I am? 
If so, what have you done to better 
manage your email life? Let me know 
at George@maximumpc.com.

WE SPEND A LOT OF TIME fussing about 
with hardware. Tinkering, tweaking, and 
otherwise trying to squeeze out every 
last drop of GPU and CPU performance. 
You can Ô¨Ånd endless amounts of 
resources that tell you what you should 
and shouldn‚Äôt do with your hardware. 
But very little attention is given to the 
operating system side of the computer, 
which is unfortunate, because that‚Äôs 
ultimately where the results matter the 
most. So we‚Äôve gone and done just that.
Windows 10 is, without doubt, one of 
Microsoft‚Äôs best releases of Windows. 
It‚Äôs well made, stable, and chock-full of 
features‚Äîsome of which you may not 
actually want or use. Take, for example, 
Cortana, the smart assistant not unlike 
Apple‚Äôs Siri, Amazon‚Äôs Echo, or Google 
Assistant. Sure, it can tell jokes, look up 
information on Wikipedia, and search 
your computer, but I personally don‚Äôt 
use it. I reckon my keyboard skills can 
get me what I need much faster. So I 
disable it, freeing up system resources 
for other duties.
Things don‚Äôt end there, though. There 
are other areas of Windows 10 that you 
can dive into, and with a little know-how, 
gain just not increased performance 
from your PC, but enough performance to 
witness actual usability improvements. 
Startup 
options, 
system 
services, 
clearing out old system update Ô¨Åles, and 
more‚Äîour crack team of experts show 
you a wide range of pro-level tricks to 
take your rig by the horns.
Some of you might be thinking to 
yourselves, ‚ÄúWell, I‚Äôve done all that, 
but tell me what‚Äôs going to give me 
giant leaps in performance on my next 
upgrade.‚Äù The answer might surprise 
you: displays.
Earlier this year, at CES, I witnessed 
a Ô¨Çurry of display announcements. 8K 
displays Ô¨Ånally debuted, with some 
companies showing working demos. 
HDR is on the rise, primarily in HDTVs, 
but it‚Äôll no doubt show up in computer 
displays this year. Refresh rates are 
going up, too, and a new HDMI spec was 
announced, delivering 4K resolution at an 
astonishing 120Hz. The display industry 
is moving forward at an ever-increasing 
rate, and this will also push the PC.
So, we took it upon ourselves to 
bring all the newest bad-ass displays 
into the lab, and run them through our 
tests. Some are better than others, 
but one result is consistent across the 
board: Current displays have advanced 
signiÔ¨Åcantly in just a few years.
Which brings me to my sage advice: 
Invest in a display. A good display is 
always the most expensive part of my 
build, because I spend all my computing 
time staring at it, and a good display will 
outlast your PC, for the most part. You 
might upgrade this and that inside, but 
the screen will be there for some time. 
What do I use? Two 32-inch 4K 
displays. But with 8K on the way, I‚Äôm 
starting to get an itch.

Commercial 3D memory Ô¨Ånally arrives
Intel‚Äôs Optane 
Technology Ships
individual transistors. These 
first modules have two layers 
of memory cells, but three-
layer cells are due later in the 
year. The exact physics and 
manufacturing method has yet 
to be revealed. 
Intel‚Äôs announcements about 
Optane veer into hyperbole. 
It will ‚Äúchange the face of 
computing with an unparalleled 
combination 
of 
speed, 
endurance, and density.‚Äù These 
first modules are about three 
times faster than a standard 
SSD in random and sequential 
read. Write speeds are less 
impressive‚Äîstill capable of 
beating SSDs, but by much 
slimmer margins. The 32GB 
module manages 1,600MB/s on 
reads and 500MB/s on writes. 
Latency is between SSD and 
DRAM. We have nothing on 
price yet, but they won‚Äôt be 
cheap. Expect to pay around five 
times the price of flash RAM, or 
twice that of DRAM.
Sure, they are fast, but 
you are right to feel a little 
underwhelmed at this point. 
This doesn‚Äôt beat emerging 
NVDIMM or NVMe technology, 
and 
isn‚Äôt 
what 
we 
were 
promised. Early presentations 
about 3D XPoint spoke of speed 
boosts of up to a thousand times 
SSD, and touched on the long-
held dream of unified RAM and 
storage. Alas, these claims have 
proved elusive. The tantalizing 
early 
proclamations 
were 
based on the core performance 
of the chips at the cell level. 
Once it goes through all the 
subsystems of the hardware and 
software, the speed is degraded 
considerably. Perhaps it might 
have been wiser not to publicize 
such appetizing claims in the 
first place. 
Larger capacity SSD versions 
are currently being tested in 
servers by IBM and Facebook. 
Before the end of the year, we 
will see more mainstream 
applications, which currently 
sport ‚ÄúBeach‚Äù codenames‚Äî
Mansion Beach, Stony Beach, 
and Carson Beach‚Äîeach a 
family of Optane SSDs running 
on PCIe or SATA. Its future as 
a DRAM replacement is less 
obvious. Conventional modules 
are already cheap and fast, but 
its use in servers cannot be 
far away.
Optane is having a slightly 
difficult birth, and the 8000p is 
a bit of a dull start. However, 
a huge research budget and 
much effort has been poured 
into its success. This is just a 
taster. A single unified high-
speed memory is still a long 
way away, but we do have 
commercial 3D memory that is 
already faster than NAND, and 
has an interesting roadmap. 
You will be hearing a lot more 
about Optane. ‚ÄìCL
THE LAUNCH of an M.2 SSD 
memory 
module 
is 
not 
normally of much interest, but 
Intel‚Äôs new 8000p is based on 
the company‚Äôs new Optane 
technology, which has been 
several years in the making, 
and responsible for quite a few 
promises along the way. The 
modules come in 16GB and 32GB 
sizes, and you‚Äôll need a specific 
set of hardware to run them: a 
Kaby Lake chip, a 200-series 
chipset, a compatible M.2 slot, 
and a compatible BIOS. They 
have been designed to boost 
Intel‚Äôs Rapid Cache Technology 
performance, and a small 
number of machines have been 
announced that are Optane-
ready, among them Lenovo‚Äôs 
ThinkPad T570 and HP‚Äôs Envy 
Curved 34.
Optane is Intel‚Äôs brand name 
for the 3D XPoint technology, 
a 3D memory system jointly 
developed by Intel and Micron. 
The tech has been in the labs 
since 2012. Memory cells sit in 
a three-dimensional mesh, and 
use the change in resistance 
of the materials to store data. 
Importantly, 
there 
are 
no 
Memory cells sit in a 3D
mesh, and use the change 
in¬†resistance to store data. 
Not quite what we were expecting, but it‚Äôs a start.

THE CHINESE MINISTRY of Industry and 
Information has announced the start of a 
14-month campaign to target the use of 
VPNs in China to ‚Äúclean up‚Äù the Internet, 
and encourage the ‚Äúhealthy‚Äù development 
of the industry. Running a VPN business 
inside China will now require a state license, 
and presumably close co-operation with the 
authorities. President Xi Jinping recently 
delivered a speech to the World Economic 
Forum on the beneÔ¨Åts of opening up 
world trade and world borders. The irony 
is palpable.
Details on the new rules are a little 
vague, but the clear targets are businesses 
inside China who provide VPNs for its 
citizens. 
The 
Ô¨Årewall 
that 
surrounds 
China has also started blocking VPN sites. 
Large corporations are unaffected so 
far. VPNs are popular in China to access 
otherwise blocked sites, such as Google and 
YouTube, and evade censorship on speciÔ¨Åc 
subjects, principally anything concerning 
dissatisfaction with the state. 
The Internet arrived in China in 1994, 
and the state started to attempt to control 
it in 1998; a Ô¨Årewall for the entire nation 
followed in 2003. China‚Äôs efforts to control 
the Internet were initially greeted with much 
skepticism in the West. The Internet was 
seen as too porous and too distributed to 
be stopped. While it is still difÔ¨Åcult to stop a 
determined individual, the state has proved 
remarkably effective at suppressing the 
curious. In China, you expect to be tracked, 
and self-censorship is natural.
Meanwhile, in the rest of the world, VPNs 
are free to use and popular, even if the 
authorities aren‚Äôt always keen on the uses 
they are put to. China has shown that it is 
possible to effectively control what it was 
once thought almost impossible to police. 
Let‚Äôs hope nobody else gets any ideas. ‚ÄìCL 
PRESS WINDOWS AND G in the latest 
Windows Creator Update, and you 
enter the much anticipated Game 
Mode. As expected, it‚Äôs mostly about 
resource allocation, speciÔ¨Åcally CPU and GPU resources to the game, getting all 
processor cores running effectively, and keeping the GPU focused on the game 
graphics. The emphasis is not so much on performance increases‚Äîthese are 
marginal, at 2‚Äì5 percent‚Äîbut to get Win 10 rigs, Xbox One, and the forthcoming 
Scorpio console to offer a consistent experience. The target standard is 900p 
to 1080p for Xbox One, and 4K at 60fps for Scorpio. The ‚ÄúGames‚Äù section in 
‚ÄúSettings‚Äù sports the Xbox logo, and here you can conÔ¨Ågure each game. Win32 
support has been conÔ¨Årmed, too, which is welcome. You also have game 
streaming via Beam, and a new Game DVR settings page. It‚Äôs about blurring the 
lines between platforms, and focusing on a consistent game experience. ‚ÄìCL

THE LAST TWO BIG PLAYERS making 3D TVs‚Äî
LG and Sony‚Äîare to drop their support with 
this year‚Äôs models. The format is dead, for the 
mass market at least. Lack of content was obvious; there was very little broadcast 3D, and 
even that has now stopped. Enthusiasm from consumers dropped off sharply, and the TV 
people‚Äôs attention has shifted to HDR and 4K. 3D content will live on, but only through VR. 
3D TVs came on the back of 3D movies, which appeared in sporadic waves since the 
1950s, each powered by new technology. The Ô¨Årst wave lasted just three years, and the 
revivals fared little better. The current dalliance with 3D movies has at least attracted some 
big budget titles, but there are signs that this is fading, too. The odds are that 3D will return 
at some point, probably using yet another technology, but for now you had better start 
hoarding those 3D Blu-rays if you want any use out of your 3D TV. ‚ÄìCL

TECH TALK
The Current State of 
Integrated Graphics
The good news is that Intel started putting more 
effort into improving graphics when it created HD 
Graphics, in 2010. The latest iteration is its 600 
series, in the new Kaby Lake CPUs, which is largely 
the same as the 500 series. A huge chunk of the 
processor is devoted to graphics‚Äîroughly one third 
of the die. But even so, performance isn‚Äôt stellar.
I ran tests on 15 modern games, and even at low 
quality settings and 1280x720, less than half were 
playable. But there is some good news in that all 15 
games rendered without any noticeable errors. Less 
demanding games such as Overwatch, League of 
Legends, Counter-Strike: Global Offensive, Dota 2, 
and many indie titles are also deÔ¨Ånitely playable.
Perhaps more interesting is what Intel has 
been doing over the past seven generations of 
Core processors. Intel more than quadrupled its 
mainstream 
integrated 
graphics 
performance 
going from Ô¨Årst-gen to third-gen Core, but since 
then, most of the desktop parts (Broadwell being 
the exception) haven‚Äôt seen much improvement. In 
raw computational power, the fastest GT2 variants 
of Intel‚Äôs HD Graphics have sat in the 400-450GÔ¨Çops 
range since Haswell‚Äôs HD 4600 in late 2012.
AMD is only slightly better, with its top APUs, like 
the A10-7890K, sitting at 887GÔ¨Çops, while lower-tier 
parts like the A8-7670K (581GÔ¨Çops) and A6-7470K 
(410GÔ¨Çops) are well off the pace. More critically, 
all of these parts, including desktop Skylake and 
THE WORLD OF INTEGRATED GRAPHICS has a long and storied history, 
most of which can be summed up as: they‚Äôre too slow and lacking in 
functionality. For years, the most ‚Äúpopular‚Äù graphics chips have been 
Intel‚Äôs, not because they were fast but because they were ubiquitous.
Kaby Lake at 440GÔ¨Çops, are less 
than half the performance of the 
GTX 1050 (1,733GÔ¨Çops) and RX 460 
(2,150GÔ¨Çops) that represent entry-
level dedicated GPUs. That‚Äôs a big 
part of why most gamers end up 
running a dedicated graphics card.
Other factors include sharing 
system memory bandwidth. Even 
with two or three times as many 
GPU cores, without increasing the 
memory bandwidth, a lot of potential 
performance is lost. An HBM2 cache 
is a potential solution, and Intel has 
used eDRAM caches with its Iris 
products, but few desktop users are 
interested in improving integrated 
graphics when a quick upgrade to 
a dedicated graphics card can more 
than double performance.
Consoles are a different matter, 
where economies of scale come 
Jarred Walton has been a 
PC and gaming enthusiast 
for over 30 years.
into play, and by targeting a closed 
platform, manufacturers can do 
more with less. The PS4‚Äôs custom 
AMD processor uses about two-
thirds of the die space on graphics, 
with the relatively slow Jaguar CPU 
cores relegated to one eighth of 
the die. The PS4 also uses GDDR5, 
with a 256-bit interface clocked at 
5,500MT/s, with over four times the 
bandwidth of a dual-channel DDR4-
2667 conÔ¨Åguration, and the PS4 Pro 
should double GPU performance.
I‚Äôm eager to see what AMD does 
with the upcoming Zen APUs. Will 
it include a high-speed memory 
cache, along with substantially 
more GPU cores? It could, and 
I‚Äôve seen rumors of an APU with 
potentially 1,024 Vega GPU cores‚Äî
twice the number found in AMD‚Äôs 
current top APUs. Combined with 
stacked memory (a single 2‚Äì4GB 
HBM2 stack, perhaps), AMD could 
end up surpassing the performance 
of its RX 460. Certainly, AMD has 
the expertise to pull this off, but 
I‚Äôm not convinced it will be enough 
to capture the interest of gamers, 
with price being a major factor. AMD 
might end up cannibalizing sales of 
its $100-200 graphics cards in order 
to sell more $100-200 APUs.
A quick upgrade to a dedicated 
graphics card can more than 
double performance.
AMD‚Äôs Kaveri APU uses half the die 
space on graphics functionality.

17
MAXIMUMPC
maximumpc.com
SPRING 2017
The Ô¨Årst big thing that offers promise is the Vulkan 
graphics API, the spiritual successor to OpenGL. As 
an open API, Vulkan has promised a cross-platform 
API for developers who want to develop for Linux, 
Windows, Mac, and even mobile devices. (The API 
also shipped with support for the modern Wayland 
graphics server.) The API spec was published in 
early 2016, and hardware released during the year 
offered support for Vulkan. However, very few 
games offered support, with most released in 2016 
opting for DirectX 11 or 12. Id Software‚Äôs id Tech 
6-powered Doom was a notable exception, showing 
the blistering-fast performance that Vulkan offered. 
Epic‚Äôs Unreal Engine 4 also supports the API.
With a high-performance, modern graphics API 
available for Linux, one would thing there would 
be a tidal wave of AAA games for Linux. That wave 
hasn‚Äôt come. A quick scan of a list of games with 
Vulkan support on Wikipedia shows eight games 
written for Vulkan. Only two of those (Dota 2 and The 
Talos Principle) are available on Linux. (Id‚Äôs Doom is 
curiously Windows-only, breaking from id‚Äôs history 
of releasing shooters for Linux as well as Windows.)
There simply aren‚Äôt many games written for 
Vulkan yet, but given time, that will change. The 
percentage of those games that are released for 
Linux might not, however. The big reason developers 
shy away from Linux development 
is understandable. The market is 
minuscule compared to the number 
of gamers on Windows, and Linux 
presents a moving target in terms 
of libraries and drivers. Anyone 
who‚Äôs broken something like Wi-Fi 
or Bluetooth support because of an 
update knows what I mean. That 
means a huge headache for very 
little return for a game dev shop. 
But there are a few new packaging 
technologies that aim to Ô¨Åx that.
Universal 
Linux 
‚Äúcontainer‚Äù 
formats are one of the most 
important new ideas for desktop 
users. They differ slightly from 
container technologies such as 
Docker, and aim to unify the Linux 
application distribution space.
These technologies promise to 
allow developers to package their 
apps with the libraries that they 
Alex Campbell is a Linux geek 
who enjoys learning about 
computer security.
Linux is a moving target in 
terms of libraries and drivers; 
a huge headache for very little 
return for a game dev shop. 
Alex Campbell
OPEN SOURCE
Vulkan Can‚Äôt Bring 
Games to Linux by Itself
FOR YEARS AND YEARS, being a Linux user meant giving up on the idea 
of playing top-tier games when they‚Äôre released. For the most part, 
that‚Äôs still true, but technologies are emerging that will make it easier 
for developers to distribute games for Linux. Unfortunately, these new-
fangled technologies don‚Äôt solve all of the underlying issues.
need. The formats allow them to 
stop chasing library updates, and 
distribute software images that are 
known to run on the vast majority of 
Linux systems without issue.
These formats also aim to 
unify distribution in the face of the 
fracturing caused by each distro‚Äôs 
package 
management 
solution. 
Debian, Ubuntu, and Mint have 
deb; Fedora and openSUSE have 
RPM; Arch and Manjaro use tar.xz; 
Gentoo builds everything from 
source because why the hell not? 
This landscape is hell for software 
distribution (and creates a lot of 
work that volunteers have to do).
Unfortunately, the three new 
competing formats‚ÄîÔ¨Çatpak, snap, 
and AppImage‚Äîsuffer from the 
same problem, in that there‚Äôs three 
of them. Hopefully, 2017 will see a 
clear winner pull ahead of the pack. 
But even if one does, and developers 
have a clearer and more stable 
platform to target, the chicken 
(penguin?) or the egg problem will 
likely persist: There aren‚Äôt many 
gamers on Linux because there 
aren‚Äôt many games for Linux, and 
there aren‚Äôt many games for Linux 
because there aren‚Äôt many gamers.
Doomed? Actually, things are 
looking up for Linux gamers, but 
there‚Äôs still much work to be done.

BY MATT HANSON
No matter how careful you are online, you can still be at risk of picking up a nasty virus 
or other malware. If you don‚Äôt want to shell out for an expensive (and resource-hogging) 
antivirus app, there are plenty of free tools to keep you protected. Far from being cut-down 
and basic, even these free programs come with plenty of features to protect your PC. But are 
they too good to be true, and how do they compare to the built-in protection that Windows 
offers? We pit three of the most popular free antivirus tools against each other to Ô¨Ånd out.
AVG AntiVirus vs. Bitdefender 
Antivirus vs. Microsoft Defender
ROUND 1
ROUND 2
Ease of Use
We want our antivirus app to 
be unobtrusive, and keep us 
protected with the minimum of 
fuss. In that regard, Windows 
Defender has a huge advantage, 
as if you‚Äôre running a recent 
version of Windows, it is already 
installed and conÔ¨Ågured. 
Downloading and installing 
Bitdefender is straightforward, 
scanning 
your 
PC 
during 
the 
process. 
The 
interface 
is streamlined and easy to 
understand, and the Autopilot 
mode means you can leave it 
alone to get on with protecting 
you. To scan speciÔ¨Åc Ô¨Åles, just 
drag and drop them. 
AVG‚Äôs interface has simple 
indications letting you know if 
your PC is protected, and a big 
button to scan your system. It‚Äôs 
not as hands-off as the others, 
but installation is simple. You‚Äôre 
encouraged to install a browser 
plugin to protect you while 
online, but it tries to change your 
homepage and default search 
engine, which we don‚Äôt like.
Tie:
Windows Defender  
and Bitdefender
Features
Defender has virus, malware, 
and spyware detection and 
removal. Real-time protection 
monitors 
downloaded 
Ô¨Åles. 
There‚Äôs no Ô¨Årewall. Cloud-
based 
protection 
defends 
against threats identiÔ¨Åed on 
other users‚Äô PCs. It comes 
preinstalled, with no ads or pop-
ups nagging you to upgrade.
The free version of AVG 
features virus, malware, and 
spyware protection, and real-
time security updates. Unlike 
Defender, it comes with a plugin 
to protect you from unsafe 
links, downloads, and email 
attachments. A gaming mode 
(that cuts down on resources 
used) is welcome, but if you 
want 
Ô¨Årewall 
or 
phishing 
protection, you must pay.
Bitdefender offers similar 
features as AVG, plus comes 
with Autopilot mode, which 
conÔ¨Ågures 
security 
settings 
on the Ô¨Çy, enables automatic 
updates, and more, with no 
annoying pop-ups, but AVG‚Äôs 
gaming mode gives it the edge.
Winner:

Every one of these AV tools

Resource Use

can

Better still, CPU use never went 
higher than 14 percent‚Äîa far

puts the PC under.

AVG

THE DOCTOR RESPONDS: The 
Doc is well versed in matters 
of hardware, but less familiar 
with Maya, and the resources 
its renderer exploits. To get you 
the best answer, the Doc went 
to his friends at Puget Systems 
in Auburn, Washington, who 
test and write about optimizing 
conÔ¨Ågurations for workstation 
applications. Matt Bach, in 
Puget‚Äôs lab, sent over the 
following guidance: ‚ÄúIf faster 
render times from Maya are 
your primary concern, the 
solution isn‚Äôt quick or easy. 
You are correct that more 
RAM likely won‚Äôt help (32GB is 
typically plenty), and as Maya 
uses a CPU-based rendering 
engine, upgrading the GPU 
won‚Äôt improve render times 
either. The weakest link in your 
son‚Äôs dual-Xeon workstation 
is the CPUs themselves. Two 
Xeons can work very well for 
rendering, as they scale nicely 
with higher core counts, but 
the E5-2609 v3 is only one step 
up from the lowest-end CPU of 
that generation. Upgrading a 
dual-Xeon setup isn‚Äôt as cheap 
as adding RAM, but it‚Äôs the only 
thing that will help signiÔ¨Åcantly.
‚ÄúFor rendering, the Xeon 
E5-2630 v3/v4 is usually the 
lowest-end dual-CPU conÔ¨Åg 
we recommend to one of our 
customers. Compared to the 
E5-2609 v3s your son has today, 
a pair of E5-2630 v3s could be 
expected to drop that 30-hour 
render to 17 hours or so. You 
could use a cheaper CPU, but 
anything lower than the -2630 
will typically be outperformed 
by a 6- to 10-core 3GHz-plus 
single-CPU system, which is 
also more affordable. If you 
have the budget to go higher 
than the E5-2630, a quick rule 
of thumb for rendering is to 
use Xeon CPUs that end in a 0 
(-2630, -2640, -2660, and so on).
‚ÄúKeep in mind that many of 
the other tasks you mentioned 
(gaming, Maya modeling, and 
all Adobe software) do not work 
well with dual-Xeon systems. 
There is extra processing 
overhead when you have two 
physical CPUs, and unless the 
software is good at utilizing 
multiple cores at the same 
time‚Äîas rendering is‚Äîyou 
often get better performance 
with one CPU than with two. In 
fact, for gaming and modeling/
animation, four cores running 
at high clock rates yield much 
better performance.‚Äù

THE DOCTOR RESPONDS: Let‚Äôs 
start with your memory 
question. The answer isn‚Äôt 
straightforward because 
it depends on a number 
of variables. First, there‚Äôs 
your motherboard‚Äôs BIOS. 
Depending on how the Ô¨Årmware 
was written, it might choose 
the slowest common memory 
settings, the memory settings 
from a preset priority list of 
slots on the board, the serial 
presence detect (SPD) settings 
from a random module, or 
it could refuse to boot when 
presented with conÔ¨Çicting SPD 
information. The memory chips 
on those modules could also be 
incompatible due to different 
densities or capacities.
So, there‚Äôs a lot that could go 
wrong‚Äîif you have the option to 
avoid mixing kits, take it.
If you already have them 
both, try installing the modules 
in alternate slots, so that the 
kits each populate one channel. 
Check your manual for guidance 
on where to put the higher-
capacity modules. Should your 
system boot successfully at 
low baseline settings, dial in 
manual settings for the data 
rate, CL timings, command 
rate, and voltage (that is, don‚Äôt 
use XMP). Specify the lower of 
the settings between both kits.
Should you run into trouble

BIOS settings to default. If the 
issue persists, you‚Äôre likely 
out of luck. Don‚Äôt be surprised 
if this is the case; it could be 
that the capacity differences 
between 16GB and 4GB 
modules are simply too great.
In regard to your external 
drive question‚Äîyes, any virus 
on one drive can potentially 
affect other drives as well.
The Right Connection
Hello, I‚Äôm a long-time

Nguyen‚Äôs Lab Notes article 
‚ÄúShort Circuits‚Äù intrigued me,

FAN and CPU_OPT headers, 
which Tuan warns against doing.
I‚Äôve had the system for a

Z170X-Gaming 7 motherboard,

‚ÄìMike H.
THE DOCTOR RESPONDS: Tuan 
was having issues with an Asus 
board; that company‚Äôs support 
folks advised against using both 
headers concurrently to avoid 
the problem he encountered.
The Doc consulted Gigabyte 
about your motherboard, and 
was told that the CPU_FAN 
and CPU_OPT headers can be 
used together without an issue. 
CPU_FAN is either for a heat 
sink or radiator fan that can 
ramp up and down based on 
CPU temperature; CPU_OPT is 
an additional header that can 
provide 100 percent power all 
the time to the pump. Each has 
its own IC to control amperage 
limits. You‚Äôre in the clear.

Never before has the high-end PC monitor 
market been so vibrant and innovative. 
Allow Jeremy Laird  to help you solve this 
particular paradox of choice 
BIG-SCREEN 
DEBUT
26
MAXIMUMPC
SPRING 2017
maximumpc.com
big screen group test

A WINDOW INTO YOUR COMPUTING WORLD. That‚Äôs 
what a PC monitor is. The question is, do you want 
your view to be a miserable, letter-box glimpse, or 
a broad, expansive, and liberating vista?
OK, that‚Äôs a loaded way of posing the problem of 
screen choice. It ain‚Äôt all about size. In fact, at the 
high end, generous proportions are a given. So it‚Äôs 
all the rest that you need to worry about: panel type, 
native resolution, and pixel pitch, for instance.
But that‚Äôs just the basics. The PC monitor market 
is more complex than ever. That begins with a much 
broader choice of shapes and sizes. You can go 
seriously big these days. You can also go seriously 
wide, thanks to 21:9 aspect ratio panels. And you 
can go curved for a wrap-around experience.
Meanwhile, several spectacular new image-
enhancement technologies have emerged over the 
last two years. It wasn‚Äôt that long ago that refresh 
rates for LCD monitors were stuck at 60Hz. Now you 
can go up to 160Hz. Gamers can also opt for dynamic 
refresh rates synched with the output of their video 
card. Then there‚Äôs the widespread adoption of far 
higher resolutions, such as 4K or UHD.
Even more recently, there‚Äôs been a revolution in 
colors and contrast. HDR, or high dynamic range, is 
the latest technology to hit the display market. It‚Äôs 
already pretty much taken over the high-end HDTV 
segment. Now it‚Äôs beginning to assimilate monitors. 
However, the process will be more complicated 
on the PC, as if the practicalities of implementing 
HDR weren‚Äôt already complex enough. Point being, 
you‚Äôll be able to pick and choose between various 
aspects of what has become known as HDR.
An example is quantum dot technology. It‚Äôs 
a color enhancement feature that isn‚Äôt strictly 
associated with increasing dynamic range, but 
it‚Äôs part of the broader HDR toolkit that‚Äôs 
Ô¨Ånding its way into PC monitors. So, 
let‚Äôs dissect the high-end PC 
display market.
27
MAXIMUMPC
maximumpc.com
SPRING 2017

LET‚ÄôS TAKE A TOUR around the recent history of 
the high-end PC monitor. Remember when a 24-
inch widescreen monitor with a 1920x1200 native 
resolution constituted high end? We‚Äôre thinking 
2004, and the Dell 2405FPW. You can get something 
close to that for about 100 bucks today. Incredible.
About three years later, Dell shook things up 
again with the 3007WFP, a 30-inch 2560x1600 beast 
that was so spectacular at the time, it was hard to 
imagine anything bigger. But a further Ô¨Åve years 
down the road in 2017, and the 3007 is toast.
Screen tech is advancing at breakneck speed. One 
moment you‚Äôre marveling at the latest superwide 
curved monitor, the next you‚Äôre having your entire 
Ô¨Åeld of view saturated by staggering HDR visuals 
courtesy of a 55-inch UHDTV, and every monitor 
you‚Äôve ever used before suddenly seems tiny.
But let‚Äôs not get ahead of ourselves. Choices must 
be made. You can‚Äôt have everything in one monitor. 
For instance, let‚Äôs say you wanted a 4K screen with 
160Hz refresh, and 40-inch-plus diagonal. It can‚Äôt be 
had. So, you need to be clear on your priorities.
Let‚Äôs look at gamer-centric tech, including ultra-
high refresh rates. High refresh on the desktop is 
nice. It‚Äôs hard to go back to a 60Hz panel once you‚Äôve 
experienced 120Hz-plus. But, arguably, anything 
above 120Hz for the desktop is redundant. Adaptive 
refresh technology, including Nvidia‚Äôs G-Sync and 
AMD‚Äôs FreeSync, is even more gamer-centric. If you 
don‚Äôt game much, you can probably ignore it.
Now let‚Äôs move on to size and shape. Your options 
are extensive and the choice subjective. It is also 
intimately related to native resolution. For starters, 
bigger isn‚Äôt always better. The UHD or 4K pixel grid of 
3840x2160 sounds impressive. On a 32-inch monitor, 
it gives a nice, tight pixel pitch, crisp fonts, and loads 
of detail. On a 50-inch panel, it‚Äôs a whole different 
ball game. At least, it is at normal PC monitor 
viewing distances. You can move further away with 
a larger panel, but if the apparent size is then the 
same, what‚Äôs the point? Likewise, is a screen hooked 
up to a PC while you sit on a couch 15 feet away a 
monitor or a TV? Similarly, it‚Äôs possible to go too 
big in terms of panel viewability. Beyond 40 inches, 
a screen situated at conventional monitor viewing 
distances requires a whole lot of head craning.
That said, many people who thought 30-inch 
panels were too big 10 years ago are probably happily 
running 32-inch or 34-inch monitors today. What 
feels unwieldy at Ô¨Årst, can soon make reverting to a 
smaller screen seem restrictive. On a similar note, 
where do projectors Ô¨Åt in? A projector can never be a 
direct replacement for a PC monitor, yet nothing can 
compete with their sheer cinematic scale.
POINTS OF VIEW
Aspect ratio is another consideration. Superwide 
21:9-aspect monitors are gaining traction. They‚Äôre 
fantastic for movies. They work great with games. 
They can be thoroughly ergonomic for viewing 
multiple documents or web pages at the same time, 
too. But they‚Äôre also restrictive in terms of vertical 
resolution and desktop space. So, while you may 
be able to view documents side by side more easily, 
your view of each is more limited, which means 
more scrolling. The 21:9 aspect is also distinctly 
sub-optimal for standard 16:9 HDTV video content.
Curved versus Ô¨Çat is yet another geometric 
conundrum. In our experience, this is a question 
of personal preference. Some people love curved 
screens, others think they look ridiculous. We argue 
that the bigger a screen is, the more sense a little 
curve can make. But just remember, 
not all screens sport the same 
curve. Some are curvier than 
others. So, head down to your 
local store, and get a feel for the 
various radii of curve on offer.
All of which brings us to plain, 
old image quality. Partly, that‚Äôs a

question of panel choice. At this end of the spectrum, 
it‚Äôs unlikely you‚Äôll be considering a TN panel, though 
some gamers prefer TN at any price, thanks to its 
superior pixel response. For everyone else, it‚Äôs a 
choice between IPS and VA panels, plus closely 
related variants thereof, such as Samsung‚Äôs PLS 
tech, which is essentially IPS by another name.
Ultimately, the traditional divisions here remain. 
IPS is the best choice for color accuracy and viewing 
angles, while VA delivers the best contrast and color 
saturation. So, you‚Äôll want IPS for a super-precise 
pro-style monitor, or VA for the ultimate in visual pop 
and punch. That said, those traditional categories 
are blurring. An IPS display with quantum dot 
backlight technology can have colors with even 
more pop than VA with a conventional LED backlight.
What‚Äôs more, the emergence of HDR, or high 
dynamic range, technology is complicating matters, 
It feels like OLED has been the 
next big thing in monitor tech for 
about a decade. It‚Äôs been one of 
those technologies that seems to 
always be just around the corner, 
but somehow never arrives.
OLED, or Organic Light 
Emitting Diode, tech has actually 
been successful on the small 
scale of smartphone displays 
for several years. Scaling 
the technology reliably and 
affordably for big displays has 
proven more difÔ¨Åcult. However, 
the major brands in the HDTV 
market went big on OLED at CES 
earlier this year. What‚Äôs more, 
the Ô¨Årst laptops with full OLED 
displays have now gone on sale, 
including the Lenovo ThinkPad X1 
Yoga and the latest Alienware 13.
Strictly speaking, the Ô¨Årst 
OLED PC monitors have also 
arrived. The Dell UP3017Q 
delivers an OLED panel in a 
30-inch 4K form factor. Problem 
is, it‚Äôs a $5,000 screen. OLED, 
then, isn‚Äôt exactly mainstream. 
But it will be worth the wait 
when it does become more 
affordable. That‚Äôs because, when 
you get right down to it, LCD is a 
fundamentally borked technology 
on which to base a large, full-
color display. When you sit in 
front of the latest 4K HDR LCD TV, 
that‚Äôs easy to forget. The world‚Äôs 
display engineers have done an 
amazing job of compensating 
for the obvious Ô¨Çaws in LCD 
technology. But the advantage 
OLED has, by virtue of each pixel 
being its own light source in 
terms of contrast, viewing, and 
response, is simply too much. 
Already, OLED beats LCD hands 
down in many areas, and it‚Äôs still 
a nascent technology. A decade 
from now, OLED displays will 
be making anything with an LCD 
panel look utterly ancient.
WHEREFORE ART THOU, OLED?
a subject we covered in depth in our January issue. 
Long story short, HDR uses technologies, including 
local backlight dimming, to dramatically increase 
the range of brightness a screen can display. 
The new HDR standards also bring with them an 
expansion in color spaces, and support for up to 12 
bits of color per channel‚Äîin the past, even high-end 
monitors have been limited to eight bits per channel.
A true HDR monitor is capable of much greater 
highs and much lower lows than a conventional 
screen. The results can be spectacular, but HDR 
may not be for everyone. A 32-inch 4K IPS monitor 
with quantum dot technology is a very different 
proposition from something like a large 4K TV with 
HDR capability, to the point where it‚Äôs more a case 
of what Ô¨Åts your needs than necessarily determining 
which is better. You pays your money. You takes your 
choice. And the end result is a stunning screen. 
Lenovo‚Äôs Yoga 
X1 is one of the 
Ô¨Årst vaguely 
affordable PCs 
with an OLED 
display.

The basics of quantum dot involve materials 
that absorb certain frequencies of light, 
convert them, and re-emit. The ‚Äúquantum‚Äù 
bit is because the semiconductor crystal 
on which it‚Äôs based leverages a nanoscale 
effect known as quantum conÔ¨Ånement, 
which 
involves 
electron 
holes, 
two-

Bohr radius. But you knew that, obviously.
Basically, it takes suboptimal light output 
from an LED backlight, and buffs it up to 
enable increased color vibrancy and Ô¨Ådelity. 
The CF791 also sports a couple of gaming-
friendly features: 100Hz refresh and AMD 
FreeSync adaptive refresh support.
Finally, it‚Äôs curved. Not just any old 
curved, but 1500R curved. That means 
the curve‚Äôs radius is a yard and a half or 
so‚Äîover twice as curved as the Philips

recover your data

53
maximumpc.com

MAXIMUMPC

BEFORE WE CAN EVEN THINK about 
doing any kind of data recovery, it‚Äôs 
worth knowing how data is stored 
on the typical drive. Every situation 
is slightly diÔ¨Äerent, of course, and 
we‚Äôre taking an incredibly basic 
look at NTFS formatting here; other 
Ô¨Ålesystems work in diÔ¨Äerent ways, 
although with the right software, 
you should be able to aÔ¨Äect a 
recovery from just about anything.
The typical drive is broken into 
partitions. These aren‚Äôt physical 
breaks, they‚Äôre logical‚Äîa certain 
portion of the drive‚Äôs bits are 
allocated, in a contiguous manner, 
to 
split 
the 
drive 
apart 
into 
individually 
managed 
sections, 
known as volumes. Information 
about these volumes‚Äîtheir size, 
location on the drive, and the like‚Äî
is stored in the drive‚Äôs Partition 
Table, which sits alongside the 
Master Boot Record (MBR) in the 
Ô¨Årst sectors of the drive. The MBR is 
accessed when you boot your PC, so 
the BIOS knows where it‚Äôs heading. 
Each volume is invisibly broken 
into more sections. The Master File 
Table (MFT) is the most important of 
these. It‚Äôs a database that contains 
information about every Ô¨Åle and 
folder on your drive, pointers to 
them, and, in some cases, even 
entire Ô¨Åles, if they‚Äôre smaller than 
about 512 bytes. Before the MFT 
is a boot sector, which holds a bit 
more speciÔ¨Åc information about 
the 
volume‚Äîmost 
importantly, 
it points to where the MFT is 
located‚Äîand, if the partition is 
both active and designated as a 
primary partition, loads NTldr to 
kick oÔ¨Ä the boot process. The same 
sector is also duplicated at the very 
end of the volume, in case the Ô¨Årst 
one goes bad, as is the MFT itself.
Sandwiched between these tiny 
sections is the Ô¨Ålesystem data 
itself‚Äîthe stuÔ¨Ä the MFT points to. 
These are your Ô¨Åles. When you start 
a fresh NTFS drive, they‚Äôre stored 
quite neatly; a few months or years 
into the drive‚Äôs life, they really are 
not. Files are placed into whatever 
space the MFT designates as free, 
and while the system makes a good 
stab at doing this sensibly, it often 
needs to break your Ô¨Åles apart, and 
spread them over several areas of 
free space, with the references to 
each of the parts stored in the MFT. 
This is why mechanical drives tend 
to slow down; the read head needs 
to jump around the drive to hit each 
bit of the thing you‚Äôre trying to load. 
SSDs don‚Äôt have this problem, since 
they can jump between sectors 
almost instantaneously (relatively 
speaking, at least).
STAYING SAFE
So what does this all mean for you? 
Well, consider this: When Windows 
deletes a Ô¨Åle, it doesn‚Äôt do anything 
to the Ô¨Ålesystem data. It merely 
scrubs the reference from the MFT, 
and allocates the Ô¨Åle‚Äôs space as 
‚Äúempty.‚Äù The data itself is still there, 
almost in its entirety. For now. If the 
MFT later chooses to store a new Ô¨Åle 
in the same space? Some or all of 
that deleted Ô¨Åle‚Äôs data will be gone.
This means the Ô¨Årst critical step 
of any data recovery eÔ¨Äort (and a 
PREVENT DATA LOSS 
Recovering Ô¨Åles is our 
aim here, but all of this 
can be avoided with a 
sensible computing 
regime. If you care 
about your data, build 
in a backup routine. The 
prevailing wisdom states 
that you should follow 
the rule of three (three 
copies of your data, 
stored on at least two 
kinds of media, at least 
one of which is kept off-
site), and it makes sense 
to get as close to this as

GPU VRAM
Memory seems to be one of the most 
over-marketed products within the PC 
enthusiast‚Äôs arsenal of componentry. 
Whether it‚Äôs SSD cache being used to 
speed up ageing hard drives, enticingly 
large supplies of GPU memory on 
a $150 card, or desktop DDR4 with 
frequencies up into the 4,000MHz 
range, it tends to be obscured by a 
barrage of baseless promises.
For example, MSI recently stated 
that by increasing DDR4 frequencies 
from 2,400MHz to 4,000MHz, you‚Äôd 
see an in-game improvement in The 
Witcher 3 of average frame rates from 
94.1 to 114.1. In tests in our March 2016 
issue, we saw zero difference in frame 
rates across 10 memory kits with a 
800MHz difference between them.
But my issue lies with graphics 
memory, and how much is ‚Äúenough.‚Äù 
We had an argument with Asus‚Äôs PR 
team regarding the appropriate level 
of VRAM for various cards. The 4GB 
1050 Ti doesn‚Äôt make sense to us, 
when the 3GB 1060 is about $30 more, 
yet performs almost three times 
better. Asus‚Äôs argument is that games 
require ever larger VRAM caches, and 
you‚Äôd be future-prooÔ¨Ång yourself by 
getting a 4GB 1050 Ti over a 3GB 1060. 
We disagree. If you game at 1080p, 
3GB of VRAM is plenty for any game 
today. Larger textures don‚Äôt provide 
any more detail, and if you‚Äôre looking 
to upgrade to higher resolutions, the 
GPU will become the bottleneck. Don‚Äôt 
fall for marketing lies‚Äîbuy a card 
with an appropriate amount of VRAM 
for the resolution and games you play, 
and build a system around that.
DON‚ÄôT INSTALL GEFORCE EXPERIENCE
For people concerned about privacy, the latest Nvidia update 
brought an unwelcome visitor in the form of required account 
creation to access any features. To counter this, head to www.
geforce.com/drivers, manually enter your conditions, and 
click to download the latest drivers as an EXE. On installation, 
deselect ‚ÄúGeForce Experience,‚Äù and you‚Äôre good to go. Check 
back monthly or whenever you hear about a new driver release.

BACKGROUND
The world of video game platforms has been changing quickly. 
Manufacturers have resorted to mid-gen refreshes to keep up 
with 4K TVs and VR headsets, and here‚Äôs the third iteration of 
the PS4 generation. We may be amateur gamers, but we‚Äôre 
revved up to tear the PlayStation 4 Pro down, professionally. 
MAJOR TECH SPECS
‚Ä¢  Eight-core AMD ‚ÄúJaguar‚Äù X86-64 CPU, clocked at 2.1GHz  
(up from 1.6GHz).
‚Ä¢  4.2TÔ¨Çops AMD Radeon-based graphics.
‚Ä¢ 8GB GDDR5 RAM and 1GB DRAM.
‚Ä¢  1TB removable and upgradable hard drive storage.
‚Ä¢  802.11a/b/g/n/ac, Ethernet, and Bluetooth 4.0 (LE).
‚Ä¢  Blu-ray x6 CAV, DVD x8 CAV.
KEY FINDINGS
‚Ä¢  The triple-decker design is a little different from the ‚ÄúÔ¨Årst‚Äù 
PlayStation 4, but that doesn‚Äôt stop us‚Äîwithout wasting time, 
we Ô¨Çip the console over, pop the Ô¨Årst panel, and hit pay dirt! A 
single, standard Phillips #0 screw stands between us and

‚Ä¢  We‚Äôre gonna have to remove the ‚ÄúWarranty void if seal 
removed‚Äù stickers to go any further. Adding insult to injury, 
the screws hidden beneath are Security Torx screws‚Äî
designed to stop you getting in. With the case cracked open, 
we can remove a large metal shield. Unfortunately, all this 
does is give us a view of the motherboard‚Äôs backside.
‚Ä¢  With the beast on its back, the opposite panel pops off after 
some prodigious prying‚Äîbut no ‚Äúwarranty void‚Äù stickers! 
Alas, removing this panel isn‚Äôt the panel-cea we were hoping 
for. We can pull out the power supply, but nothing else. While 
you can‚Äôt remove the fan, you could certainly clean it from 
here‚Äîmaybe that‚Äôs why we‚Äôre granted this non-warranty-
voiding access zone. 
‚Ä¢  Thwarted by the lack of any more screw heads on the PSU 
side of the PS4 Pro, we Ô¨Çip it back over and turn our attention 
to the motherboard. Burrowing through a layer of shielding 
behind the CPU reveals a cool X-shaped retaining bracket, 
similar to ones we found in another console. And directly 
underneath it, we dig up a plastic-and-metal cover that 
seems to be the consumer electronics version of a pizza 
saver, protecting the back of the board from the heatsink 
retaining bracket. 
‚Ä¢  Repairability Score: 8 out of 10 (10 is easiest to repair). No 
adhesive makes disassembly and reassembly easy. The 
non-proprietary hard drive is simple to access and upgrade 
or replace‚Äîand doing so doesn‚Äôt void your warranty. While 
you can clean the fan, removing it‚Äîand other components‚Äî
requires lots of (warranty-voiding) disassembly. Security 
screws and tamper-evident seals discourage users from 
disassembling and repairing their PlayStation 4 Pro. 
Remarkably simple, 
although that‚Äôs not 
really surprising.
Fortunately, you can 
still swap out the hard 
drive, a PlayStation 
staple since the PS3.
We‚Äôre a huge fan 
of easy-to-remove 
components.

PRELIMINARY STEPS
Bots have practical uses, too. They can be programed to tweet

tweet a message unless you reset it every day. It‚Äôs also possible to

>> If you Ô¨Ånd any of these possibilities intriguing, you need to set

>> In order to proceed, we need a new Twitter account with a

THE SIMPLEST DESCRIPTION OF A TWITTER BOT is a program designed to produce automated 
posts on Twitter. The most common use for bots is spam. The bot examines key words‚Äîcleaning 
products, for example‚Äîthen responds with a promotional link for people to click to be taken to the 
spammer‚Äôs website. The feature also has non-spammy uses, however. Bots can be programed to 
search for any word or phrase, and respond accordingly. One entertaining implementation of this was 
@BDZNappa, which searched for people tweeting the phrase ‚Äúover 9,000,‚Äù to which it always 
responded, ‚ÄúWHAT!? NINE THOUSAND?‚Äù Bots can also tweet from a text source, such as 
@SunTzuBot, which tweets daily quotes from The Art of War. More sophisticated bots, such as 
@JustDiedBot, actively search the Internet for source material. @JustDiedBot searches Wikipedia 
for information about recent deaths, and tweets RIP announcements as they happen. ‚ÄìNATE DRAKE
YOU‚ÄôLL NEED THIS

GOING FURTHER
There are many more possible Python projects 
you can do with Twitter, including responding to 
keywords, automatic retweets, and even grabbing values 
from web pages, such as stock market prices. Head over to 
www.raspberrypi.org/blog/tag/python/ to see some of the 
exciting projects that have been achieved with Python. 
Bots have been around for several years, and some have 
thousands of followers. Some of them simply react to words 
in tweets. For instance, anyone using the phrase ‚Äúillegal 
immigrant‚Äù can expect a response from @DroptheIBot saying, 
‚ÄúPeople aren‚Äôt illegal. Try saying ‚Äòundocumented immigrant‚Äô 
or ‚Äòunauthorized immigrant‚Äô instead.‚Äù
The @everyword bot began tweeting every word in the 
English language in 2007. It tweeted a new word every half-
hour until it completed its task in 2014, after 109,157 words. 
There are also bots that exist for political parody. 
Mentioning the words ‚Äúcommunism‚Äù or ‚Äúsocialism‚Äù provokes 
the ire of @RedScareBot Robot J McCarthy himself, who tells 
you the ‚ÄúRed Storm is rising‚Äù and to ‚Äúcircle the wagons.‚Äù
@DeepDrumpf is a Twitter bot created by MIT, which uses 
neural network technology to analyze data and post tweets in 
the supposed speaking style of Donald Trump. The developers 
claim the bot was trained via transcripts of Trump‚Äôs speeches.
The twitter bot @factbot1 makes good use of images. 
Creator Eric Drass programed the bot in response to the 
tendency of some people to believe unproven facts, provided 
they‚Äôre accompanied by an image. The bot regularly tweets 
nearly true and nonsensical facts. One such ‚Äúfact‚Äù is that the 
Canadian government derives 38 percent of its income from 
the sale of donuts. This is plausible, given the ubiquity of Tim 
Hortons caf√©s in the land of the maple leaf, but sadly false.

by values of red, green, and blue. We‚Äôre going to change this to 
CMYK, the format used in printing things like this magazine, for 
no other reason than we were messing around with the Ô¨Åle, and 
we discovered it works better this way. Head to ‚ÄúImage > Mode 
> CMYK color‚Äù [Image A] and make the change‚Äînothing should

Ô¨Åle name alters.
2
CREATE A SKIN TONE
Now we‚Äôre going to create the colors that will be used in

(we‚Äôre assuming that your photo has a person in it, because these 
are the most common subjects for the colorization treatment). 
Close the window when you‚Äôre done. 
ONCE, THE WORLD WAS BLACK AND WHITE. Created by MGM for The Wizard of Oz (a Ô¨Ålm cruelly 
overlooked at the Oscars in favor of Gone With the Wind, which invented wind), color wouldn‚Äôt become 
mainstream technology until halfway through the 20th century, and even then pockets of resistance 
lingered, where people refused to install the relevant upgrades, claiming that monochrome was 
good enough, and that they didn‚Äôt trust the free update not to give their eyes typhoid.
Pity the PC owner in this world of perpetual twilight. The twinkling LEDs in his keyboard could 
only show shades of gray, Hercules made all graphics cards (they were fairly high res though), and 
people weren‚Äôt really certain about dithering. Artifacts of this time still exist in the form of black 
and white photos, but we can bring them into the present with a little image editing. Colorizing a 
mono photo can never bring back the true colors from the moment it was taken, but it can have a 
charming look all of its own. ‚ÄìIAN EVENDEN

CMYK is used in commercial printing, and splits an image 
into cyan, magenta, yellow, and key (black), which represent 
the inks mixed on the press to create any other color. Most 
images designed to be viewed on a screen are RGB, which 
uses a mixture of red, green, and blue light, to achieve the 
same effect. Monitors, which operate in RGB, don‚Äôt always 
accurately represent CMYK colors, especially where reds 
are concerned, but a system of color proÔ¨Åles and screen 
calibrators means colors can be kept consistent across 
screens and on to the press. We‚Äôve only used CMYK here, 
because it made one step easier‚Äîyou‚Äôll want to convert back 
to RGB before you Ô¨Ånish, and not just because CMYK Ô¨Åles can 
be signiÔ¨Åcantly larger than RGB ones.

Boost Your NAS With
Link Aggregation
1
CHOOSE THE RIGHT DRIVE
If your NAS drive [Image A] 
has twin Ethernet ports, 
check its manual or specs to see 
what forms of link aggregation 
it supports. Most drives should 
offer LACP support, although 
the terminology varies between 
manufacturers: 
QNAP 
and 
Synology 
drives 
refer 
to 
‚Äú802.3ad dynamic,‚Äù for example. 
If your drive doesn‚Äôt support 
LACP, check to see whether it 
provides its own proprietary 
form of load balancing‚Äîsee the 
‚ÄúWot, No Link Aggregation?‚Äù box on the right for details.
>> If you‚Äôre in the market for a LACP-compatible drive, look for 
a higher-end SOHO or small business model‚ÄîQNAP‚Äôs TS-231+ is 
around $170 diskless, for example.
2
BUY THE RIGHT SWITCH
Most home routers don‚Äôt come with link aggregation 
support on board. The most cost-effective way to add link 
aggregation support to your home network is through a managed 
switch [Image B]. It‚Äôs important that you carefully check the link 
aggregation features offered by your chosen switch, because 
cheaper models may not have all the features you need (as we‚Äôve 
discovered to our cost). For example, TP-Link‚Äôs TL-SG108 model 
DOES YOUR NAS DRIVE come with two Gigabit Ethernet ports? When plugged into a compatible 
switch, the two ports enable you to combine the two connections using technology called ‚Äúlink 
aggregation‚Äù or ‚Äúport trunking.‚Äù Link aggregation is a catch-all term for various methods of 
providing a parallel network connection through multiple Ethernet ports. One basic use is 
to provide a layer of redundancy‚Äîif one port fails, the other can still be used. But a more exciting‚Äî 
and practical‚Äîuse for link aggregation is LACP (Link Aggregation Control Protocol).
LACP increases the throughput of your NAS drive, which means you can speed up the shoveling 
of data to and from the drive. Hurrah, you cry: double the transfer speed! Sadly, no. You can‚Äôt 
increase the speed of a single network connection using LACP, but you can increase the available 
bandwidth for multiple connections. This helps when two or more users attempt to access the drive 
at once‚Äîfor example, uploading and downloading Ô¨Åles at the same time, or attempting to run two 
movie streams simultaneously, which is why it‚Äôs often referred to as ‚Äúload balancing.‚Äù Read on to 
discover what equipment you need, how to conÔ¨Ågure it, and how to test your tweaks. ‚ÄìNICK PEERS
YOU‚ÄôLL NEED THIS
LACP-CAPABLE NAS
Look for two Ethernet ports on 
the back.

costs under $30, but doesn‚Äôt support LACP. Instead, you

T2008, which we‚Äôre using as the focus of this tutorial. If 
you‚Äôre using a different model, you need to adapt the steps 
according to your switch‚Äôs instructions.
3

image, to or from the NAS. Make a note of the transfer 
speed. Now repeat the process using two separate PCs 
or Macs, and you should Ô¨Ånd the transfer speed is roughly 
halved on each device, illustrating the current bottleneck.
4

WHY IS IT THAT YOUR PC always seems to develop problems at the most inopportune moments? 
While Windows is pretty good at Ô¨Åxing its own problems, there are still frustrating times when its 
own built-in tools, from self-healing to troubleshooters, fail to do the job.

come back, narrow it down by including more information. Include

or provide more general information, such as your version of 
Windows, hardware device, or software program.
6 
BROWSING & FILTERING LOGS
What happens if you can‚Äôt Ô¨Ånd the information you‚Äôre looking 
for? It‚Äôs likely the events you need to track are buried away. 
Manually browse the logs to narrow your search. Expand ‚ÄúWindows 
Logs‚Äù in the left-hand pane, where you‚Äôll Ô¨Ånd logs in categories. Use 
‚ÄúApplications‚Äù to search for program-related problems [Image D], 
and ‚ÄúSystem‚Äù for Windows and hardware.
>> Logs record all manner of information, much of it irrelevant‚Äî
at least when troubleshooting a problem. Click the ‚ÄúFilter Current 
Log‚Äù button in the right-hand pane to Ô¨Ålter the list a number of 
different ways: by the time it occurred, the type of event (focus on 
‚ÄúCritical,‚Äù ‚ÄúError,‚Äù and ‚ÄúWarning‚Äù), event sources, IDs, keywords, 
or any combination. Click ‚ÄúOK‚Äù when done.
E
7 
CREATE AND SAVE YOUR CUSTOM VIEW
If you want to create a Ô¨Ålter that provides you with 
information across multiple logs, or wish to create a 
Ô¨Ålter you can easily access in future, click ‚ÄúCreate Custom 
View‚Äù under ‚ÄúActions.‚Äù It works in the same way as the 
Ô¨Ålter‚Äîto monitor more than one log with your custom 
view, click the ‚ÄúEvent logs‚Äù drop-down menu, and check 
the logs you wish to include [Image E].
>> Click ‚ÄúOK,‚Äù then give your custom view a suitable 
name, and choose where to save it within the ‚ÄúCustom 
Views‚Äù tree in the left-hand pane. Going forward, you‚Äôll be 
able to access this view at any time directly from here‚Äî
it‚Äôs the perfect shortcut for targeted troubleshooting that 
takes an extended period of time to monitor and act upon.
8 
CREATE AND SCHEDULE ALERTS
Change the name to something that will help you 
recognize it in Task Scheduler, and click ‚ÄúNext‚Äù 
twice. Neither email nor ‚Äúdisplay a message‚Äù options 
work, so leave ‚ÄúStart a program‚Äù selected, and click 
‚ÄúNext.‚Äù Type ‚Äúmsg‚Äù into the ‚ÄúProgram/script‚Äù box, then 
type ‚Äú*‚Äù followed by your message (for example, ‚Äú*Error 
Alert‚Äù) into ‚ÄúAdd Arguments.‚Äù Click ‚ÄúNext > Finish > OK.‚Äù
>> Event Viewer also lets you create alerts that are 
triggered when speciÔ¨Åc events occur. This can be useful 
for troubleshooting underlying problems, as you may Ô¨Ånd 
a seemingly unrelated event is triggering the error, or get 
early warning that something nasty is about to happen. 
Once you‚Äôve located the event you wish to monitor, right-
click it and choose ‚ÄúAttach Task to This Event.‚Äù
9 
REMOVE ALERT
Eventually, you may no longer want that alert 
popping up (because you‚Äôve Ô¨Åxed the problem!). To 
remove it, open Task Scheduler‚Äîtype ‚Äúscheduler‚Äù into the 
‚ÄúSearch‚Äù box, and click ‚ÄúTask Scheduler‚Äù when it appears, 
or press Win-R, type ‚Äúcontrol schedtasks,‚Äù and click ‚ÄúOK.‚Äù 
Expand ‚ÄúTask Scheduler Library > Event Viewer Tasks,‚Äù 
right-click the alert, and choose ‚ÄúDelete,‚Äù then ‚ÄúYes.‚Äù
10 
TROUBLESHOOTING MADE EASIER
Now you‚Äôve discovered the basics of how Event 
Viewer works, you can use it to help you track 
down problems in the future. By examining your system 
logs using its interface [Image F], you should be able 
to locate the information you need to go out and not just 
diagnose what‚Äôs ailing your PC, but also hopefully Ô¨Ånd a 
solution to bring it back to good working order, too.

WITH AN INEXPENSIVE PC MICROPHONE, it‚Äôs easy to record your own podcasts, music, audio 
books, or narration for videos. The results can sound great, but by taking a little time to enhance 
them, you can bring them up to professional standards. Audacity is the perfect software for this; it‚Äôs 
powerful, but easy to use, and it‚Äôs free.
Audacity does an excellent job of cleaning up audio, but there are a few steps you can take to 
ensure you get the best possible recording quality in the Ô¨Årst place. First, make sure the input

>> If you notice background noise in your recording, select

ZAK STOREY, REVIEWS EDITOR
LENGTH OF TIME: 2-3 HOURS
Just How
s?
Many Cores?
m
SINCE THE LAUNCH of Intel‚Äôs Core 
series processors, way back in 2009, 
there‚Äôs been one simple rule associated 
with its mighty lineup of chips: If you‚Äôre 
into gaming, to avoid bottlenecks, you‚Äôre 
going to want to equip yourself with a 
Core i5, minimum. Four cores are king‚Äî
any more, and you‚Äôre wasting your hard-
earned dollar; any fewer, and you‚Äôre in 
for a world of frame rate hurt. 
However, a lot has changed since 
2009. And although the incremental 
processor performance increases Intel 
has staggered out of the gates since 
then have been somewhat lackluster, 
cumulatively 
they‚Äôve 
provided 
us 
consumers with about a 70‚Äì80 percent 
increase in computational performance.  
So we got to thinking: As game devs 
still haven‚Äôt managed to take advantage 
of the multiple cores we already have, 
does that old saying still ring true? Do 
you still beneÔ¨Åt from having a quad-
core, high-clocked i5, over a dual-core 
i3? Or have those IPC improvements 
lead us into an age when you can get 
away with a cheaper Pentium for all your 
1080p gaming needs? With Kaby Lake 
Ô¨Ånally introducing Hyper-Threading on 
its Pentium range, allowing for dual-
core and four-thread budget offerings, 
we decided to put it to the test in this 
quad-optioned mid-range build.
THE CONCEPT
LEVEL OF DIFFICULTY: MEDIUM 
Is it time to ditch the old gaming adage?

MAXIMUMPC
maximumpc.com

CORE VARIANCES 
DUE TO PAGE LAYOUT RESTRAINTS, we can‚Äôt put all four variants 
of our system in the table on the right. However, as it‚Äôs only the 
processor that changes during testing, we‚Äôll list them here. 
Our base system features the Pentium G4600 listed in the table, 
with two cores, four threads, and a 3.6GHz clock speed (no turbo) 
for a total of $1,236. The next uses Intel‚Äôs Core i3-7300, with two 
cores and four threads, still sans turbo, packing an impressive 
4.0GHz, taking the total to $1,278. The next step up is substantial: 
the Core i5-7600K, a four-cored, four-threaded turbo behemoth, 
capable of hitting 4.2GHz at stock, and bumping the price up to 
$1,361. And, Ô¨Ånally, the Core i7-7700K. Four cores, eight threads, 
and a 4.4GHz maximum turbo brings the total skyrocketing up to 
$1,438‚Äîa difference of $202 from Pentium to Core i7.
We decided to take advantage of Corsair‚Äôs Carbide 270R. This 
budget chassis checks all the boxes for our mid-range build. It‚Äôs 
accessible, comes with just enough hard drive support to keep 
us happy, and provides a clean-looking aesthetic inside and out. 
Cooling support is strong, and the addition of a clean PSU cover 
made it a no-brainer. For GPU, we chose Nvidia‚Äôs GTX 1060 6GB. 
We could have gone with an AMD RX 480 8GB, but the GTX 1060 
provides more frames per dollar than the RX 480, so it wasn‚Äôt a 
tough call to make. On top of that, two SSD hard drives and 16GB 
of DDR4 pull this beauty right into 2017 with a bang.
COOLER MASTER‚ÄôS LATEST 240MM AIO was a headache, 
as it deviates from the traditional Asetek mounting 
design. With most coolers, you secure the backplate to 
the motherboard using double-ended screws of varying 
sizes, depending on whether you‚Äôre attaching it to a 2011 
board, a 1150/51 board, or an AMD offering. Then you 
place the CPU block on the four screws, and secure it 
with four thumb screws. With the MasterLiquid Pro, 
Cooler Master has removed the screw between the 
board and the backplate, so you have to position the 
backplate through the board, with long pre-attached 
screws. Then, while holding the backplate in place, 
you position the block on the screws. Finally, while 
somehow holding it so it doesn‚Äôt move, you use the 
thumb screws to secure it. Pro tip? Find a buddy or, as 
we did, literally use your head to hold the block in place.
ONE OF THE FEW COMPLAINTS we have about Corsair‚Äôs 
270R stems from the uppermost fan mounts‚Äî
primarily that they lack any form of dust cover. Being 
an awkward size and not supporting 360mm radiators 
either, regardless of its dimensions, makes things even 
more frustrating when you‚Äôre on the hunt for a dust 
Ô¨Ålter. Your best bet, if building in this particular chassis, 
would be to use two 120mm fans to blow air out of the 
top of the chassis. This should at least prevent too much 
dust entering your system while it‚Äôs running, although it 
will need some loving maintenance to keep it truly clean 
in the long term.

ALTHOUGH CORSAIR still doesn‚Äôt have quite as clean and 
beautiful a PSU cover as a lot of its competitors, it‚Äôs damn 
good to see one included here. Three-quarter sized, it 
provides plenty of ventilation for multiple orientations of 
power supply, and easily lets us hide the vast majority of our 
excess cables below. One thing that still haunts us from our 
water-cooling days, however, is fan orientation. We always 
advise that you install your power supply with the fan facing 
down wherever possible, because a leak from the AIO or a 
GPU block, for instance, is likely to kill it.
WE WENT ALL-OUT with this little build to get the cable 
management just right. Using a plethora of cable ties, we 
secured what we could to the back of the chassis, to make sure 
airÔ¨Çow wasn‚Äôt restricted, and to ensure that the rear side panel 
was easier to replace‚Äîand, to be honest, to keep everything 
looking a little tidier. Take your time on this bit: Figure out which 
cables go under what, and where you can hide stuff, without it 
being too much of a major issue.

1
Although you can‚Äôt see it from the 
front, it‚Äôs entirely possible to mount 
two 3.5-inch hard drives in the back. Strip

2
We had issues with the fans running 
in push conÔ¨Åguration in this chassis. 
Cooler Master‚Äôs fan design allows the fan 
blade unit to move forward, eventually 
scraping against the chassis at higher 
speeds. We added rubber spacers to stop

3
Gigabyte‚Äôs Z270X-Ultra offers 
extra spacing between front panel 
headers, making it easy to identify where 
to install your power cables. Additionally, 
we never install the HD audio pass-
through, because quality generally tends 
to be far worse than via the rear I/O.
WELL, THERE YOU HAVE IT FOLKS: a double 
whammy mini feature buried within Build It. 
So, is this the end of the road for Intel‚Äôs Core i5 
series processor? Probably not. Legacies are 
always difÔ¨Åcult to quash, and just as AMD‚Äôs 
Ryzen has one hell of a stigma to overcome 
in the mind of the average PC enthusiast, so 
does the concept of the Core i5 not being the 
king of gaming.
Take a glance at the Internet, and you‚Äôll see 
dozens of commentators purporting that the 
Core i5 is the way to go, and that even going up 
to the Core i7 isn‚Äôt worth the cash, because you 
only gain 10 percent performance in games. 
The reality, on the other hand, is very different. 
Across the board, from one of Intel‚Äôs lowest 
$80 chips all the way up to its mainstream 
rendering powerhouse at $350, there‚Äôs 
absolutely no difference in game. It seems 
those IPC improvements have been making a 
difference to the ecosystem after all‚Äîjust not 
in the way we might think.
Building this particular system was actually 
considerably frustrating‚Äîmostly due to the 
cooler being so incredibly annoying to install. 
The backplate, coupled with the fans scraping 
the front of the chassis, and the pipes being 
far too resistant to movement, really gave us 
pause for thought as to whether this was the 
right cooler for us. We even had to unplug and 
reinstall the PWM pump fan header at Ô¨Årst, 
because it refused to pump water around 
the system, forcing our Intel Core i7, that we 
tested Ô¨Årst, to install Windows 10 on to the 
SSD at 99‚Äì100 C, at a paltry 200MHz. Although 
it was certainly impressive to witness, it was 
also excruciatingly frustrating.
Cooler problems aside, it turned out to be 
a pretty clean-looking build. Keeping all the 
storage around the back of the motherboard 
tray actually makes a world of difference. And, 
although the PSU cover is only a 75 percent 
case Ô¨Åt, it does leave plenty of room for 
radiators and cooling up front, and it grows 
on you after a while. Is there anything we 
would change? Well, if this were genuinely 
a build for purchase, not designed for that 
wide range of processor testing, we would 
probably drop the 1TB SSD in favor of an HDD, 
potentially go down to a B250 motherboard to 
save a bit of cash, use a lower specced PSU, 
and potentially even 8GB of DDR4, depending 
on budget constraints. That would bring 
this particular build down to around $630, 
including shipping. 
THE END OF THE ROAD FOR THE CORE i5?

ABOUT SIX MONTHS AGO, we put the giant 
blue box that was Digital Storm‚Äôs Aventum 3 
desktop to the test. It was a beastly gaming 
machine, but heavy as hell, and just as 
expensive. We‚Äôre thinking of the Velox as 
the Aventum‚Äôs little brother, and for a little 
more than half the cost, it performs nearly 
as well as its elder sibling.
A big part of the Aventum‚Äôs $8,500 price 
tag was due to its Hyper-Threaded Core i7-
6950X, which checks in at $1,700. The Velox 
makes up for skipping on the enthusiast-
class CPU route thanks to Intel‚Äôs new Kaby 
Lake processors. Our unit was outÔ¨Åtted 
with a Core i7-7700K, overclocked to 
4.9GHz, one of the highest desktop core 
clocks we‚Äôve seen on a prebuilt system. 
While it doesn‚Äôt quite keep up with the 
6950X in Cinebench R15 or x264, which 
really take advantage of Hyper-Threading, 
the 7700K was hot on the Aventum‚Äôs heels 
in PCMark 8 Creative, with a score of 9,404. 
To complement the Kaby Lake CPU, the 
Velox features an Asus Maximus IX Hero 
Z270 motherboard. Slotted in there is 32GB 
of DDR4-2666 RAM, and a 512GB Samsung 
960 Pro SSD, connected via NVMe. For 
additional storage, the 960 Pro is backed 
up by a 3TB Toshiba HDD. A Corsair H115i 
liquid cooler keeps CPU temperatures 
down, while the case is outÔ¨Åtted with 
Thermaltake Riing fans for everything 
else. Finally, graphics are handled by a 
pair of GeForce GTX 1080s, outÔ¨Åtted with 
EVGA‚Äôs ACX 3.0 cooling solution.
Size-wise, the Velox‚Äôs all-metal chassis

WITH THE VAST MAJORITY of boards, as far 
as average users are concerned, providing 
little beyond greater connectivity and 
expandability, forking out the cash for 
Ô¨Çashier mobos often makes little to no 
sense. 
Performance 
differentials 
are 
almost non-existent, and taking a GTX 1080 
and a Core i7-7700 from a $70 B250 mobo 
all the way up to the $300 kings of Z270 
will likely provide practically no difference 
beyond increased connectivity, better 
looks, and a Ô¨Çashier UEFI BIOS. Unless 
you‚Äôre on the liquid-nitrogen cooling train, 
with a full-Ô¨Çedged arsenal of Core i7s 
beside you, you‚Äôre unlikely to see much 
difference, as the PC ecosphere tends to 
lend itself more to silicon limits within the 
processors themselves than anything the 
motherboards can muster.
So, Gigabyte‚Äôs Z270X-Ultra Gaming 
impressed us from the get-go. On the 
outside, it certainly looks like a high-
end choice. A quick glance, and you‚Äôll see 
brushed black aluminum heatsinks, the 
now ever-present RGB lighting, a rear I/O 
shield, and a cornucopia of connectivity, 
including a single M.2 PCIe x4 slot, a U.2 
connector, a bank of four SATA ports 
(including SATA Express), two separate 
SATA 6Gb/s ports, and two internal USB 
3.0 headers as well. No doubt a premium 
offering, then? Well, perhaps not‚Äîthe 
A badass budget-busting bombproof board
Gigabyte Z270X-Ultra  
Gaming
Z270X-Ultra Gaming is currently available 
for a meager $165. That‚Äôs $30 less than the 
ITX board we picked up from MSI this issue, 
and half the price of MSI‚Äôs overclocking 
pro Z270 XPower Gaming Titanium we 
reviewed the issue before.
Does it show? Sort of, but not in the 
places you might expect. Performance 
across the board was actually pretty 
impressive. At stock, it easily outpaced 
every single motherboard we‚Äôve tested 
to date, scoring a pleasing 21.16fps 
in X265, and a staggering 995 points 
consistently in Cinebench R15. Memory 
latency was also top-notch, coming in at an 
incredible 54.0ns.
However, it‚Äôs not all plain sailing, and 
it‚Äôs only when you start looking at how the  
board handles voltage, in particular, that 
you realize just where it‚Äôs coming unstuck. 
By default, the Ultra Gaming auto-Ô¨Åxes 
CPU Vcore voltage between 1.3 and 1.35V‚Äî
at most, 0.1V higher than Intel‚Äôs prescribed 
settings, and more than 0.05V higher than 
most other competitors. Not only does this 
improve general stability, but it also allows 
Gigabyte to optimize CPU performance 
across the board at stock.  The downside, 
however, is that it draws notably more 
power from the wall under load, with our 
Ô¨Ågures showing 156W maximum, with only 
the XPower coming close out of the Ô¨Åve 
boards we‚Äôve now tested, at 131W. And, 
alas, that isn‚Äôt the end of the Ultra‚Äôs voltage 
problems. During our overclocking spree, 
and similar to all the boards previously 
tested, it still managed to achieve the 5GHz 
silicon limit, but at a noticeably higher 
voltage than the others, at 1.33V, with 
only the Z270i Gaming Pro Carbon coming 
close, at 1.32V. Undervolting also failed to 
impress, with the Gigabyte again being the 
highest, at 1.12V at stock.
Overclocking is only a small part of 
the package, of course, and although 
tweaking voltages can reduce overall 
processor temperatures, it‚Äôll probably be 
an afterthought for the majority of people. 
Voltages aside, the plethora of connectivity 
options, fantastic aesthetics, strong stock 
performance, and price make the Z270X-
Ultra Gaming phenomenally good value 
for money. And similar to the offerings 
Gigabyte mustered last generation, we 
can‚Äôt see many, if any, mobo manufacturers 
ousting Gigabyte from the top spot of best 
bang for buck just yet. ‚ÄìZAK STOREY

IN THE EARLY NOUGHTIES, an interesting 
concept arose from our fervent pursuit 
of 
more 
powerful 
technology: 
As 
transistor size shrank, efÔ¨Åciency per watt 
increased, and die size fell, manufacturers 
aggressively pushed for smaller and 
smaller devices. The ITX form factor was 
developed in 2001 by VIA Technologies as 
a way of shrinking desktop computers to 
a more manageable size, and it has since 
become a staple for enthusiasts looking to 
build a tiny desk-sitting powerhouse.
MSI‚Äôs Z270i Gaming Pro Carbon is the 
Ô¨Årst in the latest line of Kaby Lake boards 
we‚Äôve seen that deals with ITX. And we‚Äôll 
be honest: Although the on-board support 
is as good as, if not better than, it has ever 
been, the rear I/O gives us pause. This 
isn't limited to MSI, though‚ÄîGigabyte, 
Asus, and ASRock have all stripped down 
the rear I/O of their ITX offerings, and put 
about 25 percent less stuff on it. Because, 
you know, ITX system users have fewer 
peripherals, or something. Yeah, we 
don‚Äôt get that logic either. Anyway, let‚Äôs 
compare the Z97i Gaming AC against the 
Z170i Gaming Pro and the Z270i Gaming 
Pro Carbon. The Z97i has four USB 3.0 and 
Z270 ITX oÔ¨Äering
four USB 2.0 connections, giving a total of 
eight. The Z170i has four USB 3.1 and two 
USB 2.0, for a total of six. And the Z270i has 
three USB 3.1 and two USB 2.0‚Äîa total of 
just Ô¨Åve. It sounds like a petty complaint, 
but look at the plethora of peripherals that 
connect via USB, and it‚Äôs easy to see how 
quickly those connections can be taken 
up. A mouse, a dual-port keyboard with a 
pass-through, a webcam, a USB headset‚Äî
that‚Äôs Ô¨Åve ports taken up immediately. Got 
a wireless dongle? Too bad. A wireless 
controller? Not any more. 
PIN POINTS
USB woes aside, MSI‚Äôs Z270i Gaming Pro 
Carbon is a solid board. It‚Äôs nice to see the 
ITX layout get Ô¨Ånalized, with 24-pin and 
8-pin power getting concrete placement, 
and SATA and USB being more accessible 
than before. The reinforced DDR and PCIe 
ports, although laughable, do give you a 
little more assurance‚Äîthe weight of a GPU 
isn‚Äôt going to shatter them. And the M.2 
PCIe x4 slot on the back keeps us happy 
when it comes to super-fast storage.
Performance 
was 
consistent, 
as 
always, with little variance between many 
of the benchmarks. Memory latency took a 
hit, and our X265 benchmark was the worst 
of the boards we‚Äôve tested so far, but that 
was made up for by the lower power draws. 
Overclocking was a little under par‚Äî
our lowest undervolt from stock was 1.1V, 
the highest of the boards we‚Äôve tested, and 
we managed 5GHz at 1.32V, compared to 
the 1.3V of last issue‚Äôs more overclocking-
oriented XPower Titanium. It is a solid 
offering‚Äîit‚Äôs just a shame that the rear I/O 
is shrinking at such a rate. ‚ÄìZAK STOREY

SOLID-STATE STORAGE is one of the wonders 
of the modern world. Thinking about it, 
we‚Äôre reminded of the immortal words of 
Arthur C Clarke, the great science Ô¨Åction 
writer. He reckoned that any sufÔ¨Åciently 
advanced technology seems like magic.
Go back 100 years, and what would 
anyone make of, for instance, a 256GB 
MicroSD card? The notion something so 
tiny could store hundreds of thousands of 
books would surely seem like witchcraft. 
Nowadays, it‚Äôs pretty impressive that you 
can bag a 128GB USB stick for under $25.
Despite that, SSDs aren‚Äôt yet the default 
option for mass storage. That will happen 
eventually. But not yet, not even thanks to 
Crucial‚Äôs 2TB MX300. It‚Äôs about as close 
as you‚Äôll get to a really big SSD aimed at 
a mainstream audience. At $549, however, 
it‚Äôs getting on for 10 times the price of the 
cheapest 2TB magnetic hard drive.
Of course, the MX300 still majors on 
capacity rather than performance. That‚Äôs 
because it‚Äôs a SATA drive, rather than a PCI 
Express model, which brings limitations in 
terms of the peak bandwidth on the SATA 
interface and the inefÔ¨Åciencies of the AHCI 
protocol it uses. The latter never being 
designed with solid-state storage in mind.
Thus, we‚Äôre talking peak claimed 
performance around 500MB/s for reads 
and writes, and IOPs a fair bit below 
100,000. The 2TB MX300, incidentally, 
has the same claimed performance for 
sequential throughput and IOPs as the 1TB, 
The cheapest  
2TB SSD in town
750GB, and 525GB MX300 models. Only 
the entry-level 275GB model differs with 
slightly lower performance. It also shares 
the familiar Marvell 88SS1074 controller 
with the rest of the MX300 family.
Similarly, this 2TB model uses the 
same 384Gb TLC 3D Ô¨Çash memory dies 
as previous MX300s. It‚Äôs that unusual 
capacity per die that leads to the MX300 
range having odd sizes, with this drive 
serving up 2,050GB. The MX300 range also 
has a dynamic write acceleration mode 
that switches a portion of the memory to 
SLC mode for increased performance. For 
the 2TB model, the amount of memory that 
can be switched to SLC mode is increased. 
The MX300 also comes with Crucial‚Äôs 
SSD management software, which enables 
you to tweak settings and performance. 
Among other features, you can update the 
Ô¨Årmware, monitor the health of the NAND 
chips, and toggle the Momentum Cache 
feature, which uses system memory to 
buffer random writes.
If all that reads like a feature list without 
much real-world analysis, the truth is that 
the MX300‚Äôs real-world performance isn‚Äôt 
all that interesting. SATA SSDs such as 
this are a fairly mature technology, and 
the limitations we mentioned mean there‚Äôs 
zero chance of this drive setting any new 
records. What you want is a reliable drive 
with no performance nasties. That‚Äôs what 
the 2TB MX300 largely delivers.
In our synthetic performance tests, it 
delivers pretty much exactly what you‚Äôd 
expect, with peak performance around 
500MB/s, and 4K results in the mid-20MB/s 
area for reads, and 120‚Äì140MB/s for 
writes, depending on the benchmark app 
in question. It‚Äôs a similar story of generic 
SATA drive performance in our real-world 
compression and copy benchmarks. All of 
which means the MX300 ultimately trades 
on price, which is handy, because it‚Äôs 
comfortably the cheapest 2TB SSD you can 
currently buy. ‚ÄìJEREMY LAIRD
