IT‚ÄôS VERY LIKELY end of days for Intel‚Äôs CPU 
strategy as we know it. We don‚Äôt mean that 
in an apocalyptic sense. Intel isn‚Äôt about to 
suffer an existential calamity. But AMD‚Äôs 
Ryzen processor is coming, and we‚Äôre 
conÔ¨Ådent it‚Äôs going to shake things up.
That‚Äôs the context into which Intel‚Äôs 
Pentium G4600 arrives. Based on the 
latest Kaby Lake microarchitecture, it‚Äôs 
very much a child of the last Ô¨Åve years 
or so, when Intel ruled on the high seas, 
across the land, and up in the air. Within 
that narrative, the fact that Intel has 
bequeathed the G4600, plus two other 
new Kaby Lake Pentiums, with Hyper-
Threading capability constitutes news.
Hyper-Threading is the ability for each 
CPU core to process two software threads 
in parallel. And it‚Äôs been baked into every 
Intel Core processor since Nehalem in 
2008. Actually, it‚Äôs rumored that it was also 
in the Core 2 Duo, just not enabled. And, of 
course, it was Ô¨Årst seen in the Pentium 4 
Netburst chip way, way back in 2000.
The point is that it‚Äôs always in any 
desktop CPU model you buy from Intel; it‚Äôs 
just enabled or disabled to help cook up a 
few different chip models. It‚Äôs the sort of 
ruse you can get away with when you have 
little to no competition‚Äîa situation that 
prevails today, but isn‚Äôt going to last.
Anyway, the Pentium G4600 slots in at 
$82, and offers up two Hyper-Threading 
enabled 3.6GHz cores, supported by 3MB 
of cache memory. And that‚Äôs pretty much it 
Hip, hip, hooray for 
Hyper-Threading
for the CPU side of things. There‚Äôs no turbo 
mode, and the CPU multiplier is locked, so 
overclocking is essentially a non-starter.
Like all mainstream desktop CPUs, the 
processor cores are only half the story‚Äî
literally, as integrated graphics make up 
almost half of the G4600‚Äôs 14nm CPU die. 
In this case, it‚Äôs an Intel HD Graphics 630 
GPU, at the top end of the smaller of the 
two graphics cores Intel is sticking in its 
Kaby Lake chips. It has 24 execution units 
to the 48 of the Iris Plus cores. So, even by 
integrated standards, it‚Äôs nothing special. 
But for $82, you aren‚Äôt getting special.
What you are getting is very good 
single-threaded performance. The G4600 
cranks out 151 points in Cinebench R15 in 
single-threaded mode. The Core i7-7700K 
manages 182. Put another way, if all you 
were interested in was single-threaded 
performance, and overclocking wasn‚Äôt in 
your vocabulary, there‚Äôd be little reason to 
spend more than 82 bucks on a G4600.
Flick the multi-threading switch, on 
the other hand, and those numbers jump 
to 385 and 970. The 7700K is only a quad-
core chip, of course. The 10-core Core i7-
6950X monster will spew out about 1,750 
points in Cinebench. Then again,  it costs 
about 15 times as much. Arguably, then, 
what matters is that for general-purpose 
computing, the G4600 gets the job done. 
It feels perfectly zippy for web browsing, 
playing back HD video content, and most 
anything else you‚Äôre likely to do day to day.
It even turns in decent numbers in 
games, showing that the age-old problem 
of coding games to scale beyond a few 
cores remains. Just don‚Äôt try to use it to 
re-encode hours of 4K video or render a 
pro-level 3D scene. In the current reality, 
then, the G4600 is appealing. But change 
is in the air, and the measure by which CPU 
value is assessed could be very different 
within just a few weeks. ‚ÄìJEREMY LAIRD
Intel Pentium 
G4600

THIS IS NOT THE FIRST time Razer has 
unleashed the Kraken, and it‚Äôs not the 
Ô¨Årst time we‚Äôve faced it down. Its Ô¨Årst go-
around, when we reviewed the shiny white

exactly impress; its bass-above-all-else 
sound stage was a bit much, and we had 
trouble with the mic. Can this 7.1 model, 
which sits alongside the newly-tuned Pro 
V2, offer us something more palatable? 
We‚Äôll address the issues we had with 
the original shortly‚Äîlet‚Äôs begin with the 
beneÔ¨Åts (or not) that the 7.1 offers over the 
Pro model. The most obvious difference is 
the lack of a four-pole jack, consigning the 
7.1 to USB-based use only. There‚Äôs also a 
bit of added virtual surround technology, 
Razer‚Äôs Chroma LED lighting, a MEMS

Like the V1 before it, this Kraken is a 
mighty aluminum monster‚Äîlight, strong, 
and padded to within an inch of its life. It 
has even fatter circumaural ear pads‚Äî
replaceable with oval models, which would 
suit larger ears, but only if you‚Äôre willing to 
pay for the upgrade‚Äîand despite its snug 
Ô¨Åt, this is a headset that you can wear 
from dawn to dusk without worrying about 
squeezing or discomfort. 
The 7.1 virtual surround is, frankly, a 
bit weird. It‚Äôs highly adjustable through 
the app, but so subtle that it might as 
well not be there. When you do happen to 
catch a moving sound, it feels as though 
the Kraken is painting more of a vertical 
than horizontal picture, a disconcerting 
sensation, which throws one out of the 
game somewhat. The Chroma lighting, 
similarly, might as well not be there, 
seeing as you‚Äôll ideally be wearing this 
headset rather than looking at it. It‚Äôs all 
about making a statement to those around 
you; if you‚Äôve been dying to state ‚Äúmy ears 
are glowing purple,‚Äù more power to you. 
Here‚Äôs something the Kraken 7.1 V2 
does oh-so-right, though: its 5V-powered 
microphone. 
Retractable 
into 
the 
earphone and perfectly poseable, the 
microelectromechanical, 
fully 
silicon-
based construction of the mic pulls in a 
Exploring the 
depths of bass
clear, rich, quality sound that‚Äôs leagues 
above any tiny electret in this price 
bracket. The noise canceling is decent, too. 
This is an utterly, utterly impressive piece 
of gear. We‚Äôd like to see MEMS spread 
all the way across the mid-level headset

isn‚Äôt silly pretty lights‚Äîto settle for USB-

BASS CAMP
That‚Äôs one check in the box, then. So 
what of that all-important sound stage? 
We were a bit concerned when Razer 
described it as ‚Äúlouder‚Äù than its older 
sibling, which we diagnosed with acute

on the bass. Let‚Äôs begin with the good

nothing missing, and a rich, satisfying level

The microphone is out of this world, the

STEELSERIES: a legend in the hardware 
world. Its reputation is only trumped by 
its own new products supplanting itself 
on the way to the top of the peripheral 
hill. The company is neither the huge 
production foundries of Logitech, churning 
out hundreds of thousands of products 
each year, nor the niche-audience-slaying 
design houses of Mionix or BenQ Zowie, 
but a happy mix of the two. And that 
balance speaks volumes. With a legacy 
encompassing products such as the 
Sensei ambidextrous gaming mouse, the 
HV3 lineup of headphones, and even the 
Ô¨Årst ever Rival, SteelSeries has deÔ¨Ånitely 
cemented its place in our hearts when 
it comes to those additional little input 
methods we use on our prized systems. 
That said, mouse development seems 
to have come to a grinding halt, certainly 
as far as innovation is concerned. With 
high-dpi optical sensors now perfected, 
RGB lighting mastered, and a plethora 
of mice in a variety of shapes, sizes, and 
button complements, it‚Äôs hard to imagine 
where the pointy rodent will go next. Well, 
that‚Äôs what we thought, until two mice 
in particular caught our attention in the 
last 12 months: Mionix‚Äôs vital-statistics-
tracking Naos QG, and SteelSeries‚Äôs 
extravagant Ô¨Çagship, the Rival 700. We 
didn‚Äôt manage to get the latter into the mag 
at the time‚Äînot for lack of trying, mind 
you. We initially had our hands on a sample 
in the ofÔ¨Åce way back in April 2016, when 
we thought it was absolutely fantastic. 
However, due to SteelSeries‚Äôs concerns 
with the braided cables being too ‚Äúgrippy,‚Äù 
the company (frustratingly) delayed the 
release, and took the mouse back to the 
drawing board for another four months, 
to redesign the cable‚Äîyep, SteelSeries is 
that serious.
THINKING OUTSIDE THE BOX
So, here we are at last, with the mythical 
beast sitting in front of us. The SteelSeries 
Rival 700. We rarely speak about the 
unboxing experience in our reviews, but 
with the Rival, it‚Äôs something else. You get 
a small rectangular box, split diagonally. 
Opening it up on its hinge reveals nothing 
Perfection in non-
ambidextrous form
more than the mouse itself and a small 
cardboard box. You‚Äôd be forgiven at this 
point for thinking that the Rival 700 is a 
wireless mouse‚Äîit‚Äôs only when you dig a 
little deeper that you begin to understand 
what‚Äôs going on. In short, the choice of 
cable rests with the consumer. You get 
one six-foot braided cable, one six-foot 
plastic-coated cable, plus a plastic-coated 
three-foot cable. On top of that, you can 
also swap out the cover on the rear belly 
of the mouse to either a glossy black or an 
anti-sweat soft-touch variant instead ($15 
for the pack). 
And then, of course, there‚Äôs the sensor. 
By default, the Rival 700 comes with 
an optical PixArt 3360, pumping out a 
whopping 16,000 dpi if needed, with zero 
hardware acceleration. However, if optical 
is simply not your jam, you can grab 
yourself the PixArt 9800 laser module 
instead for $25. Simply unscrew the four 
Phillips-head screws underneath, place 
your new sensor inside, and wham-bam-
thank-you-ma‚Äôam, you‚Äôre all set.
Customization aside, there‚Äôs a few little 
extras that really make the Rival 700 stand 
out. You can‚Äôt quite seen the OLED screen 
in the above shot, but it enables you to slap 
any number of small GIFs or images on 
the side of the mouse, alongside in-game 
notiÔ¨Åcations‚Äînot 
particularly 
useful, 
granted, as you should be looking at the 
screen. But what‚Äôs most impressive is the 
inclusion of rumble feedback‚Äîyou can 
assign tactile feedback to any of the seven 
buttons, in a variety of ways. Although not 
hugely useful for the two main buttons, 
having a tactile response from whichever 
button you press helps train your brain to 
use buttons that you would otherwise get 
confused about, making it one neat and 
surprisingly useful addition. The vibrations 
themselves 
are 
exceedingly 
precise, 
and don‚Äôt cause any jitter in your mouse 
positioning. We bound this feature to one 
of our focus buttons in Sniper Elite V3, and 
had no trouble picking off targets at our 
usual rate.
All in all, the SteelSeries Rival 700 is a 
well-tuned, customizable gaming mouse 
at the pinnacle of its Ô¨Åeld, designed to 
incorporate 
absolutely 
everything 
in 
today‚Äôs gaming mouse market. It does cost 
$80, which is a lot for a pixel-pointer, but 
it‚Äôs well worth it in our eyes. ‚ÄìZAK STOREY

WE LIKE ASHAMPOO. It‚Äôs hard to hold anything 
against a software company whose slogan

no doubt be wondering why Ashampoo has

Something to do with 17 being an unlucky 
number, apparently, and anyone who‚Äôs felt 
better for noticing that a building lacks a 
13th Ô¨Çoor will surely sympathize with that. 
So, what‚Äôs here that‚Äôs worth paying 
for? Free apps tend to be limited in scope,

burning of data folders to a disc, and the

that tunes a disc especially for the model of 
For those who haven‚Äôt dumped the optical drive
stereo in your car. Now, we‚Äôve never had a

Ford in mind, but we‚Äôre talking about MP3 
CDs here, and not all car stereos want the

thoughtful inclusion. The app also includes

Movies get a similar level of attention 
to detail‚Äîwith MPEG-4, H.264, and AAC 
added to the list of supported formats, the 
masterpieces you shoot on your cell phone

told) is merely a happy accident. The app

It‚Äôs also a backup app. Large amounts

discs, thanks to disc-spanning, and there‚Äôs 
compression 
and

too. The main reason you‚Äôd want to do this

we‚Äôre working on communicating with some

to their promise). That‚Äôs the kind of long-

hard drives.
You‚Äôre mainly buying convenience and 
peace of mind when you purchase an app 
like this. With so many functions bundled 
into one package, you‚Äôd have to skip across 
several free apps to get the same level of 
service, and deal with a dearth of updates, 
or poor coding, or a lack of compatibility 
with disparate hardware. If disc-burning 
is still something that‚Äôs important to you, 
Burning Studio 18 is packed full of features 
and works well. Anyone else should just buy 
a big USB drive. ‚ÄìIAN EVENDEN

hilarious to dress up as a Nazi, rather than a 
gritty wartime tactics game.
In doing this, Shadow Tactics feels like 
something new, even if it‚Äôs just the latest 
point on a line that stretches back to the 
original Syndicate. It would be easy to 
imagine this harsh but mostly fair game in 
third-person, √† la Hitman, but we‚Äôre glad it‚Äôs 
remained the way it has. ‚ÄìIAN EVENDEN

JARRED WALTON, SENIOR EDITOR
I‚ÄôVE BEEN HEARING about AMD‚Äôs Zen, now 
Ryzen, for what feels like forever. The Zen 
architecture was Ô¨Årst mentioned in 2012, 
and ever since, the repeated refrain of AMD 
CPU apologists has been ‚Äújust wait for Zen.‚Äù 
That wait is Ô¨Ånally over, and Ryzen is now 
available. But as we go to print, I still don‚Äôt 
know exactly what AMD‚Äôs Ryzen lineup will 
be in terms of core counts, clock speeds, 
and pricing. It‚Äôs quite the contrast compared 
to AMD‚Äôs open discussion of the Ryzen 
architectural details, not to mention Intel‚Äôs 
routinely leaked product roadmaps.
Here‚Äôs what I do know. AMD is planning 
on a full release of Ryzen processors, all 
multiplier unlocked. Ryzen uses a quad-
core module as its building block, with 
SMT providing eight threads, similar to 
Intel‚Äôs Hyper-Threading, and 8MB L3 cache. 
AMD claims a 40 percent improvement 
in IPC compared to its existing Excavator 
architecture, with clock speeds of at least 
3.7GHz on the eight-core part. Architectural 
changes include a reworked, more efÔ¨Åcient 
pipeline. Each core is a six-wide superscalar 
design (four-wide fetch), compared to the 
previous four-wide design, the branch 
prediction is more accurate, and cache 
latencies and bandwidth are both improved. 
The result is that Ryzen should be much 
more competitive with Intel‚Äôs CPU offerings.
Interestingly, AMD apparently won‚Äôt have 
a six-core model; instead, it will have an 
eight-core chip without SMT as its mid-tier 
offering. That means that at each product 
segment, AMD will deliver double the cores/
threads as Intel. Intel will almost certainly 
maintain its lead in per-core performance, 
but if 4C/8T Ryzen SR3 goes up against 
Core i3‚Äôs 2C/4T design, or 8C/8T Ryzen SR5 
versus Core i5‚Äôs 4C/4T, even with slightly 
lower clock speeds, Ryzen should come out 
ahead in many benchmarks. We‚Äôll have the 
full skinny next month.
Double the cores, double the fun, but no six-pack
AMD‚Äôs Ryzen 
Looks Promising
There‚Äôs this trend in the PC 
space called ‚Äúgaming.‚Äù What 
does that mean? Contrary 
to what you might think, it‚Äôs 
hardware that puts aggressive 
aesthetics and RGB lighting 
before 
useful 
innovation. 
Manufacturers are marketing 
the 
living 
diodes 
out 
of 
hardware, and attaching the 
word ‚Äúgaming‚Äù to just about 
anything. Unsurprisingly, a 
Why don‚Äôt we include the price 
of the operating system in our 
builds and system features? 
It‚Äôs a question that often 
comes up in the ofÔ¨Åce. For 
the most part, we shy away 
from the decision, because 
the needs of the individual vary 
massively across all of our 
readers‚Äîsome use Ubuntu, 
others Debian, Windows 7, 10, 
and so on. That said, if you‚Äôre 
lot of ‚Äúgaming‚Äù gear doesn‚Äôt 
do anything for actual gaming 
performance. It just means 
you‚Äôre going to pay more for 
something made cheaply, to 
cash in on the gaming train. 
This isn‚Äôt about choice or 
taste. It‚Äôs about the deÔ¨Ånition 
of gaming. Give me quality and 
performance over marketing 
hype and gimmicks any day. I‚Äôll 
take the cost savings, too.
resolutely stuck on Windows, 
but don‚Äôt want to cough up

WE HAVE A PENCHANT at MaximumPC for 
high-quality audio. Although there are 
some pretty snazzy gaming headsets, 
they tend to meander well into the $250-
plus range for anything close to decent 
sound reproduction.
Turn your head to audiophile offerings 
without a microphone, however, and 
you can easily Ô¨Ånd pairs for under 
$150 
(Audiotechnica‚Äôs 
ATH-M50Xs, 
for example). For the vast majority, 
no mic is no problem, but for gamers, 
lacking a way to communicate with your 
buddies can be a deal breaker. And for 
those unwilling to chunk out for a $200 
dedicated desktop microphone, there‚Äôs 
not a huge deal of choice.
Except, maybe there is. Antlion saw 
this gap in the market, and jumped in 
with this fantastic modular microphone.
Available in two models, the 4 and the 
5 (the 5 has both a four-pole and a USB 
adapter), the ModMic attaches to the 
side of any headset with a small adhesive 
magnetic clip. Don‚Äôt need the mic today? 
Pull it off your headset, and place it in 
its faux leather case. Sound quality is 
impeccable, and the mic supports up to 
24-bit 192KHz without worry, delivering 
some fantastically crisp and clean sound; 
not too bass-heavy, not too tinny. The 
adhesive sticker is easy to remove, and 
it comes with a variety of clips to secure 
the ModMic‚Äôs cable to your standard 
headphone cable, too. There‚Äôs very little 
to dislike‚Äîwe just wish it rotated a bit 
better on that magnetic mount. ‚ÄìZS 
$50, www.antlionaudio.com

TRON V9.9.0
Being my family‚Äôs on-
call tech support, I‚Äôve 
run into my fair share of 
systems compromised 
by a pile of bloatware, 
unnecessary toolbars, and viruses. Most 
of us know the standard system recovery 
utilities‚ÄîCCleaner, Malwarebytes, and their 
ilk‚Äîbut there‚Äôs an even better option, before 
going nuclear, and reinstalling the OS. 
Tron is an open-source script that 
automates the full process of cleaning and 
disinfecting a Windows system. First, it 
preps your system, creating a restore point, 
and running tools and scripts that ensure 
the rest of the process runs smoothly. Next, 
it runs cleaning utilities such as CCleaner 
and BleachBit, followed by de-bloating, 
ditching OEM bloatware, toolbars, and other 
pre-installed apps (excluding useful stuff 
such as Calculator and Paint). Disinfection 
is handled by Malwarebytes, KVRT, and 
Sophos, while a series of scripts check and 
repair Windows. Finally, Tron updates and 
optimizes your system, patching Adobe, 
Java, and whatever else needs updating.
It‚Äôs an impressive list of tasks that should 
take care of almost any problem. Even 
better, it‚Äôs a single download that, once 
started, runs itself‚Äîthough it can take six to 
eight hours to Ô¨Ånish.
Free, www.bmrf.org/repos/tron
DISKDIGGER  
My smartphone recently 
decided that it no longer 
liked my microSD card. 
I can‚Äôt remember if it 
was after a restart or 
update, but my screen displayed a prompt 
saying that the Samsung microSD wasn‚Äôt 
recognized, and would I like to try to format 
it? An offer that the phone couldn‚Äôt deliver 
on, as it appeared to have been corrupted. 
Long story short: No matter how many 
machines, adapters, tools, and OSes I tried, 
I couldn‚Äôt get the card to play nicely again. 
A dead microSD card is hardly the stuff 
of nightmares, particularly as the data that 
really matters was automatically uploaded 
to my Google account. Even so, it‚Äôd be good 
to take a look at what‚Äôs on the card before I 
consign it to the big trash bin in the sky. 
The problem is that Android 6.0 has 
changed the way it handles SD cards, so 
plugging the card into Windows (or Linux) 
reveals nothing. So I turned to Ô¨Åle recovery 
tools to see what was lurking beneath its 
seemingly blank surface. One tool that did 
well, and was free to try, was DiskDigger‚Äîa 
portable app that scans for a selection of 
Ô¨Åles, and allows you to save them off with 
minimum fuss. It took a while to scan the 
entire card (30 minutes for 32GB), but did 
recover a few MP3s and a couple of log Ô¨Åles.
$15, www.diskdigger.org

REVIEWS EDITOR ZAK STOREY 
RESPONDS: The fan situation 
was a mixed bag. I knew 
I wanted to use Corsair‚Äôs 
ML120s, because I wasn‚Äôt 
massively fond of Corsair‚Äôs 
old-school SP series, due 
to the excessive noise they 
produced. The ML120s drop 
well below 500rpm, and 
produce Noctua-rivaling 
figures, so they were 
definitely my preference.
Originally, due to the 
lack of available airflow in 
the S340 Elite, I intended 
to run the Kraken X52 in a 
push-pull configuration, 
like you said. I actually went 
out and bought another 
two ML120 Pros‚Äîbut just 
the standard ones. Theory 
being, I could place them in 
push configuration, at the 
front of the radiator, out of 
sight, and then have the LED 
variants pulling air through, 
while on display. 
Unfortunately, the cable 
tidy bar NZXT includes 
stops you from installing 
an additional pair of fans 
inside the chassis, because 
the bar sticks out at an odd 
angle at the top, preventing 
the radiator or fans coming 
back any further. On top of 
that, you can‚Äôt mount them 
outside the chassis in the 
inch of space given by the 
front panel for airflow, as 
you‚Äôll suffocate them, and 
there‚Äôs also the magnetic 
dust filter in the way, too.
I‚Äôm a bit particular when 
it comes to cooling, and 
prefer all the fans to be the 
same model, as (I like to tell 
myself) it gives me a greater 
understanding of the noise 
and airflow I‚Äôm passing 
through the system. I also 
love consistency when it 
comes to aesthetic decisions 
like that. So, I‚Äôm not actually 
using push-pull, but if I 
could, I would.

a business user, with some

turkey.

EXECUTIVE EDITOR ALAN 
DEXTER RESPONDS: There‚Äôs 
no doubting the appeal 
of running your own 
cloud server, but getting 
a server up and running 
isn‚Äôt always easy. Even 
with a detailed tutorial 
that runs through the main 
installation, there‚Äôs going to 
be problems‚Äîin order for 
it to work, there are certain 
dependencies that need to 
be sorted, such as a working 
database to operate from, 
a web server to integrate 
with, and a whole host of 
permissions to sort out. 
While it looks like it‚Äôs file 
ownership that has tripped 
you up, the fact that you‚Äôre 
getting a ‚ÄúNo such file or 
directory‚Äù error suggests 
it‚Äôs the previous step that 
has failed. Just to recap 
the process: You need to 
download the latest version 
of OwnCloud from https://
owncloud.org/install, making 
sure you get the OwnCloud 
server build, the one that 
has the ‚Äútar.bz2‚Äù extension. 
Next, go into a terminal 
window, and make your 
way to wherever you‚Äôve 
downloaded it, then unpack 
that tarball with:
 $ sudo tar -C /var/www/html 
 -xf owncloud-9.1.3.tar.bz2
Make sure the files are 
unpacked by browsing to 
/var/www/html/, and 
making sure there is an 
‚Äúowncloud‚Äù directory, and 
there are files inside it. Only 
then can you change the 
ownership of those files. 
We ran through the 
whole tutorial again, just to 
make sure (using a laptop 
running Ubuntu 16.04, which 
forced us to do a few things 
differently), but it definitely 
does work. So keep going, 
and good luck!

was eligible for activation of

is ridiculous. There was 
nothing left in the machine 
to identify the old computer.

was basing his complaint 
on an OEM license, which 
is not transferable to a 
new computer. The idea 
that he considered this an 
upgrade is baffling. I have 
done upgrades with a fresh

key, and Win 10 activated 
automatically because it 
recognized the computer.
Your magazine caters to 
advanced computer users, 
and your readers expect 
advanced technical info and 
advice.

your frame rate without 
making everything look

game-speciÔ¨Åc optimization

that generally provide 
the least ‚Äúbang for your
